Testimony by Accomplices in Murder and Debt Cases
Ruling on testimony by accomplices in murder and debt cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding testimony by accomplices and apply the same reasoning to analogous cases.

Core Idea
An accomplice's testimony against his interest is accepted by Muhammad but not by Abu Yusuf.

This lesson examines two scenarios where parties who are themselves liable testify that a co-party has been discharged. In both cases, Imam Muhammad accepts their testimony while Abu Yusuf rejects it, revealing a key methodological difference within the Hanafi school. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the rules of testimony in criminal and financial disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
In both cases, two of three liable parties testify that the third has been pardoned (by the guardian) or discharged (by the creditor).
The witnesses are co-liable, so they have a potential interest in the outcome.
2
State the ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad accepts the testimony; Abu Yusuf rejects it.
The same ikhtilaf applies to both the murder and debt cases.
3
Analyze the rationale
Muhammad: The testimony is against the witnesses' own interest (they remain liable) so they are credible. Abu Yusuf: The witnesses may collude to free the third, so their testimony is suspicious.
This is a classic example of istihsan vs. strict qiyas.
4
Note the exception for the debt case
If the debtor witnesses have already paid their share, the testimony is accepted by agreement because they no longer have a stake.
This clarifies that the ikhtilaf only applies when the witnesses still have an interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وها هنا مسائل أخرى : الأولى ثلاثة قتلوا رجلاً فشهد اثنان منهم على أن الولي عفا الثالث تقبل عند محمد لا عند أبي يوسف. عن الثانية ثلاثة عليهم دين شهد اثنان منهم على الدائن بإبراء الثالث فعلى الخلاف إن كانا لم يقبضا وإلا فلا اتفافاً. الثالثة شهد اثنان من الورثة على الباقي بأن هذا ابن الميت تقبل. الرابعة شهد الكفيلان بالعهدة على البائع بأنه قبض الثمن أو أبرأ المشتري منه لم تقبل كما في الخانية . مسألة الشهادة بالعفو لو شهدوا أنه عفا عنا قال الحسن : تقبل إذا قال اثنان في عفا عنا هذا الواحد فتقبل في حق الكل. وقال أبو يوسف: تقبل في حق الواحد في الخانية. ونظير هذه ما في الخانية أيضاً : لو قال إن دخل داري أحد فعبد حر فشهد ثلاثة أنهم دخلوها قال أبو يوسف : إن قالوا دخلناها جميعاً لا تقبل وإن قالوا دخلنا ودخل هذا تقبل. وسأل الحسن بن أبي يوسف عنها فقال : إن شهد ثلاثة بأنا دخلناها جميعاً تقبل، وإن شهد اثنان لا تقبل. فقال له الحسن: أصبت وخالفت أباك ا هـ.
And here are other issues: First: Three men killed a man, and two of them testified that the guardian pardoned the third. It is accepted according to Muhammad, not according to Abu Yusuf. Second: Three men owe a debt; two of them testified to the creditor that the third was discharged. The ruling follows the same difference if they had not yet received payment; otherwise, no, by agreement. Third: Two of the heirs testified to the remaining heir that this is a son of the deceased. It is accepted. Fourth: Two sureties for liability testified against the seller that he received the price or that the buyer was discharged. It is not accepted, as in al-Khaniyyah. Issue: Testimony of pardon: If they testify that he pardoned us, Hasan said: It is accepted. If two say, 'He pardoned us and this one,' it is accepted for all. And Abu Yusuf said: It is accepted for the one, in al-Khaniyyah. An analogous case in al-Khaniyyah: If someone said, 'Whoever enters my house is free,' and three testified that they entered it, Abu Yusuf said: If they say, 'We all entered together,' it is not accepted; if they say, 'We entered and this one entered,' it is accepted. And Hasan ibn Ziyad asked Abu Yusuf about it, and he said: If three testify, 'We all entered together,' it is accepted; if two testify, not accepted. Then Hasan said: You are correct and you contradicted your father. End quote.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents several legal scenarios (masa'il) involving testimony by parties who have a stake in the outcome. The first case: three murderers, two testify that the guardian pardoned the third. Imam Muhammad accepts this testimony, meaning the third is no longer liable for qisas, but may still owe blood money? Abu Yusuf rejects it. The second case is analogous: three debtors, two testify to the creditor that the third is discharged. The same ikhtilaf applies if they have not yet paid; if they have paid, then acceptance is agreed. The third case: two heirs testify that the remaining heir is the son of the deceased, thereby affecting inheritance shares; it is accepted. The fourth: two sureties (kafilaan) testify that the seller received payment or discharged the buyer; this is not accepted because they are trying to free themselves from liability. Then a separate issue: testimony of pardon (afā 'annā). If witnesses say 'he pardoned us', meaning the victim pardoned all of them, there is a difference: Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Hanafi scholar) says it is accepted if two say it, and it extends to all. Abu Yusuf restricts it to the one testifying. An analogous case from al-Khaniyyah: a man says 'whoever enters my house is free'; if three testify they all entered, Abu Yusuf initially says not accepted because they are testifying for themselves, but after query from Hasan ibn Ziyad, he clarifies that if three testify together, it is accepted; if only two, not accepted. This shows the Hanafi method of reasoning and internal differences.

Key Terms
عود (awd): Blood money, indemnity paid to the heirs of a murder victim.
كفيل بالعهدة (kafil bi al-uhdah): A surety who guarantees liability or return of goods; 'uhdah' refers to liability for defects in a sale.
إبراء (ibra'): Discharge or release from a debt or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what is the ruling when two of three killers testify that the guardian pardoned the third?
A) Not accepted because they are accomplices.
B) Accepted, and the third is no longer liable.
C) Accepted only if they are relatives.
D) Not accepted unless they have witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, testimony by an accomplice against his own interest is accepted by Imam Muhammad but rejected by Abu Yusuf when it benefits a co-accomplice.

Testimony by Heirs and Sureties: Accepted and Rejected Cases
Ruling on testimony by heirs and sureties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where testimony by involved parties is accepted (heirs) and rejected (sureties).

Core Idea
Heirs' testimony about lineage is accepted, but sureties' testimony to free themselves is not.

This lesson presents two contrasting rulings on testimony by parties who have a stake. The first accepts heirs' testimony that another person is a son of the deceased, affecting inheritance. The second rejects sureties' testimony that the seller received payment, as they seek to escape liability. These rulings illustrate the principle that testimony against one's own interest can be accepted, but testimony that benefits oneself is suspect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
First case: two heirs testify that a third person is a son of the deceased. Second case: two sureties testify that the seller received payment or discharged the buyer.
2
Note the ruling for each
First case: accepted. Second case: not accepted.
The rulings are agreed upon, no ikhtilaf.
3
Explain the rationale
Heirs' testimony reduces their own inheritance share, so it is against their interest, hence credible. Sureties' testimony frees them from liability, so it is in their interest, hence rejected.
This follows the general principle that testimony against interest is accepted.

Testimony of Pardon and the Entry Case: Internal Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on testimony of collective pardon and its analogue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ikhtilaf between Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Yusuf regarding testimony of a collective pardon and apply the analogous reasoning to the entry case.

Core Idea
Testimony of 'he pardoned us' is accepted for all according to Hasan, but only for each individual according to Abu Yusuf.

This lesson explores the testimony of witnesses who claim that the victim pardoned them collectively. Abu Yusuf restricts the effect to the testifying witnesses individually, while Hasan ibn Ziyad extends it to all. The same principle is illustrated in an analogous case involving entry into a house where freedom is promised. These cases refine the understanding of how collective testimony operates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
Witnesses say 'he pardoned us' (collective testimony). The question is whether it covers all witnesses or only those who utter the statement.
2
State the ikhtilaf
Hasan ibn Ziyad: accepted for all. Abu Yusuf: accepted only for the one testifying.
The difference is in the scope of the testimony.
3
Examine the analogous entry case
A man says 'whoever enters my house is free.' If three testify collectively 'we all entered,' Abu Yusuf initially rejects, but after query from Hasan, he accepts if three testify together.
This shows a refinement of Abu Yusuf's position through dialogue.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Hasan confirms that Abu Yusuf's revised position is correct and notes that it contradicts his earlier view.
This demonstrates the dynamic nature of fiqh and the importance of scholarly discourse.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
The Testimony of the Effeminate (Mukhannath): Definition and Ruling
Commentary on the matn ruling regarding whose testimony is rejected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for accepting the testimony of a mukhannath and distinguish between natural effeminacy and sinful behavior.

Core Idea
Testimony of a mukhannath is accepted unless he engages in evil deeds like sodomy.

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between a man who naturally has soft speech or delicate limbs and one who deliberately imitates women or commits sodomy. While the former's testimony is valid, the latter's is rejected due to fisq (sinfulness). This lesson unpacks the definitional boundaries set by the classical commentators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term and Its Context
The key term is al-mukhannath. It appears in the chapter of testimony along with other disqualified witnesses. What is the legal significance of this category?
The matn states that the mukhannath's testimony is not accepted – but this requires clarification.
2
Locate the Distinction in the Commentary
The commentator specifies that the rejection applies only to the mukhannath who does 'evil deeds' (al-rady min al-af'al). He distinguishes two types: one who naturally has softness in speech and limbs, and one who sinfully imitates women or commits sodomy.
Find the exact phrasing in the source: 'والمخنث أي لا تقبل شهادته ومراده المخنث في الرديء من الأفعال'.
3
Examine the Definitions from Multiple Commentaries
Read the quotes from al-Hidayah, al-Mughrib, al-Binayah, and Fath al-Qadir. Note that evil actions include sodomy and deliberate imitation of women in dress and speech. Natural effeminacy is not sinful.
The sources distinguish by the nun's diacritic: kasrah (natural) vs. fatha (sinful).
4
Apply the Distinction to a Scenario
Given a man with a soft voice and limp wrists, but no evidence of sinful behavior, is his testimony accepted? Apply the rule: yes, because the condition of rejection is evil actions, not appearance.
This is the practical outcome (thamarah) of the definition.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Other schools may define mukhannath differently. For example, the Shafi'i school may consider any effeminate behavior as fisq and thus reject testimony of all mukhannath unless they repent. Recognize the legitimate ikhtilaf.
The Hanafi position is nuanced, focusing on actual sinful deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات باب من تقبل شهادته ومن لا تقبل
١٤٣
لشريكه فيما هو من شركتهما والمخنث والمغنية والنائحة والعدوّ إن كانت عداوة دنيوية
مفلساً.
واعلم
منهم
وهي
أن
وعن
الأفعال لأنه قوله: (والمخنث) أي لا تقبل شهادته ومراده المخنث في الرديء من فاسق، فأما الذي في كلامه لين وفي أعضائه تكسر فهو مقبول الشهادة؛ كذا في الهداية. وفي المغرب: المخنث في عرف الناس هو الذي يباشر الرديء من الأفعال أي أفعال النساء من التزين بزينتهن والتشبه بهن في الفعل والقول فالفعل مثل كونه محلاً للواطة، والقوا مثل تلیین كلامه باختياره تشبيهاً بالنساء؛ كذا في البناية. وفي فتح القدير من أبواب الإمامة : المخنث بكسر النون وفتحها فإن كان الأول فهو بمعنى المتسكر في أعضائه المتلين في كلامه تشبيهاً بالنساء، وإن كان الثاني فهو الذي يعمل به لواطة ا هـ
Book of Testimonies, Chapter: Whose testimony is accepted and whose is not. For his partner in what is of their partnership, and the effeminate, and the female singer, and the wailing woman, and the enemy if the enmity is worldly. ... And know from them ... because [the author] said: (the effeminate) meaning his testimony is not accepted, and what is meant is the effeminate who engages in evil deeds. As for he who has softness in his speech and limpness in his limbs, his testimony is accepted; thus in al-Hidayah. And in al-Mughrib: the effeminate in common usage is he who engages in evil deeds, i.e., the actions of women, such as adorning himself with their adornments and imitating them in action and speech. Action such as being a place for sodomy, and speech such as softening his speech voluntarily imitating women; thus in al-Binayah. And in Fath al-Qadir from the chapters of Imamate: al-mukhannath with a kasrah on the nun and also with a fatha; if the former, it means he who is lax in his limbs and soft in his speech imitating women; if the latter, he is one who commits sodomy.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It explains the category of the mukhannath (effeminate) whose testimony is rejected. The matn states that the mukhannath, along with others, is not an acceptable witness. The commentator clarifies that the rejected mukhannath is specifically the one who engages in evil actions (al-radi' min al-af'al), such as imitating women in dress and speech, or committing sodomy. However, if a man merely has a naturally soft voice or delicate limbs without engaging in sinful actions, his testimony is accepted. The commentary cites al-Hidayah, al-Mughrib, al-Binayah, and Fath al-Qadir to define the term. This distinction is important for judges and witnesses to uphold the integrity of testimony.

Key Terms
المخنث (al-mukhannath): The effeminate man; in this passage, defined by engaging in evil deeds (actions of women) such as sodomy or voluntary imitation of women.
الرديء من الأفعال (al-radi' min al-af'al): Evil deeds; specifically, actions of women like adorning, imitating in speech/action, and sodomy.
عدالة (adala): Moral uprightness; a condition for the acceptance of testimony. A fasiq lacks adala.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the testimony of a mukhannath (effeminate man) accepted?
A) Always, regardless of behavior
B) Never, regardless of behavior
C) Only if he has softness in speech and limpness in limbs without engaging in evil actions
D) Only if he repents from sodomy

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts testimony of a mukhannath with natural effeminate traits; only the one who commits evil deeds is rejected.

Ruling on Testimony of a Female Singer and Wailing Woman
Explanation of why testimony of female singer and wailing woman is rejected due to committing haram acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a female singer and a wailing woman, and identify the exception for the wailing woman's own calamity.

Core Idea
A wailing woman's testimony is accepted only in her own calamity; her hire for others' misfortunes taints her 'adalah.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the testimony of two specific individuals: the female singer (al-mughanniyah) and the wailing woman (al-na'ihah). The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that their testimony is rejected. The commentary from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that the reason is their engagement in prohibited acts, as the Prophet (ﷺ) forbade two foolish sounds. However, the wailing woman's testimony is accepted when she wails over her own calamity, as her income is not tainted by seeking wealth from others' misfortunes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn ruling and the commentator's expansion
Start with the matn's simple ruling: testimony of female singer and wailing woman is rejected. The commentator expands by giving the reason: they commit a prohibited act.
2
Understand the proof
The commentator cites the hadith forbidding two foolish sounds: the sound of the wailing woman and the singer. This establishes the prohibition as the basis for rejection.
Hadith source not specified in text; implication of strong evidence.
3
Distinguish between the two cases
Note that the female singer's testimony is rejected even when she sings alone because raising her voice in song is haram, unlike men who are only restricted if they sing for people. For the wailing woman, the rejection is specific to wailing over others' calamities for money.
This is a key distinction between genders.
4
Apply the exception
Recognize that the wailing woman's testimony is accepted if she wails over her own calamity, as that act is not prohibited. This exception is clearly stated in the commentary.
The exception is based on the absence of seeking wealth from others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمغنية والنائحة) لارتكابهما محرماً لنهيه عليه الصلاة والسلام عن الصوتين الأحمقين النائحة والمغنية أي صوت النائحة والمغنية ووصف الصوت بصوت صاحبه. أطلق المغنية فشمل ما إذا كانت تغني وحدها لأن رفع صوتها حرام بخلاف الرجل، قيده بأن يغني للناس. وأطلق النائحة وهي مقيدة بالتي تنوح في مصيبة غيرها لارتكابها الحرام طمعاً في المال فتقبل شهادة النائحة في مصيبتها. وفي القاموس : ناح الرجل بكى واستبكى غيره
His saying: (the female singer and the wailing woman) because they commit a prohibited act, due to his prohibition, peace be upon him, regarding the two foolish sounds: the wailing woman and the singer, i.e., the sound of the wailing woman and the singer, and he described the sound by its owner. He used [the term] female singer generally, so it includes when she sings alone because raising her voice is prohibited, contrary to the man, and he restricted it to singing for people. And he used [the term] wailing woman generally, and it is restricted to she who wails over a calamity of others, due to committing the prohibited act seeking wealth; so the testimony of the wailing woman is accepted regarding her own calamity. And in al-Qamus: naaha means he wept and made others weep.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling from the matn that the testimony of a female singer (al-mughanniyah) and a wailing woman (al-na'ihah) is rejected. The reason is that they engage in prohibited acts: the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) forbade two foolish sounds — the sound of a wailing woman and a singer. The female singer is rejected whether she sings alone or to people, because her raising her voice in song is haram. The wailing woman is rejected when she wails over someone else's calamity for money; however, if she wails over her own calamity, her testimony is accepted. The commentary cites al-Qamus for the linguistic meaning. This ruling upholds the principle that a person who habitually commits major sins (fisq) loses 'adalah (moral integrity) necessary for testimony.

Key Terms
المغنية (al-mughanniyah): A female singer; includes any female who sings, whether alone or to others.
النائحة (al-na'ihah): A wailing woman who laments over a deceased person for money (professional mourner).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the testimony of a wailing woman (na'ihah) accepted?
A) It is never accepted under any circumstances
B) It is accepted only when she is paid
C) It is accepted when she wails over her own calamity
D) It is accepted if she wails over a stranger's calamity

Key Takeaway
The testimony of a professional female singer and wailing woman is rejected due to haram acts, but the wailing woman's testimony is accepted if she wails over her own calamity because she is not sinning.

Ruling on Testimony of an Enemy with Worldly Enmity
Detailed discussion on testimony of an enemy with worldly enmity, including a hadith and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony of an enemy with worldly enmity and explain the conditions under which it is accepted or rejected, citing the relevant hadith.

Core Idea
Worldly enmity alone does not disqualify testimony; only if it leads to sin or partiality.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the testimony of an enemy. You may think enmity automatically invalidates testimony, but the Hanafi school distinguishes between worldly enmity (which may be permissible if the witness remains just) and religious enmity (which actually indicates righteousness). The passage navigates between the opinions of the early imams and later scholars, providing a balanced approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling from the matn
The matn states that testimony of two enemies is not accepted if their enmity is worldly. Note the qualification: 'duniyawiyyah' (worldly) excludes religious enmity.
The ruling is conditional: it applies only to worldly enmity.
2
Understand the distinction between worldly and religious enmity
Religious enmity (e.g., against a disbeliever) does not disqualify; it indicates righteousness. Worldly enmity is problematic because it may arise from sinful motives.
Examples: Muslim vs. non-Muslim, heir vs. killer, husband vs. wife for adultery.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's position vs. the later scholars
Abu Hanifa is reported to accept testimony of an enemy if the witness is 'adl (upright). Later scholars (mutakhkhirin) restrict acceptance to cases where the enmity does not cause fisq, bring benefit, or ward off harm. This is the mufta bihi.
The text says 'our teacher said it is sound and upon it reliance'.
4
Examine the hadith and its interpretation
The hadith 'No testimony of one with rancor (dhi ghimr)' is cited. The Hanafis interpret 'ghimr' as rancor that leads to fisq, restricting the hadith to those who are not upright.
This reconciles the hadith with Abu Hanifa's view: the hadith applies to fasiq witnesses.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its evidence
The relied-upon position: testimony of an enemy with worldly enmity is accepted as long as the witness is 'adl and the enmity does not cause fisq, benefit, or harm. Evidence includes the hadith interpreted restrictively and the practice of the Companions.
Ibn Wahban chose this view, and it is supported by the mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والعدوان كانت عداوة دنيوية) أي لم تقبل شهادة العدو لأجل الدنيا لأن المعاداة لأجلها حرام فمن ارتكبها لا يؤمن كالموصى عفا عنا وعن له ؛ كذا في شرح الوهبانية .اهـ.
من التقول عليه. قيد بكونها دنيوية للاحتراز عما إذا كانت دينية فإنها لا تمنع لأنها تدل على كمال دينه وعدالته ، وهذا لأن المعاداة قد تكون واجبة بأن رأى فيه منكراً شرعاً ولم ينته بنهيه بدليل قبول شهادة المسلم على الكافر مع ما بينهما من العداوة الدينية، والمقتول وليه على القاتل، والمجروح على الجارح والزوج على امرأته بالزنا ذكره ابن وهبان. وفي خزانة المفتين والعدو من يفرح بحزنه ويحزن لفرحه وقيل يعرف بالعرف ا هـ. ومثال العداوة الدنيوية أن يشهد المقذوف على القاذف والمقطوع عليه الطريق على القاطع، وفي إدخال الزوج هنا نظر فقد صرحوا بقبول شهادته عليها بالزنا إلا إذا قذفها أولاً، وإنما المنع مطلقاً قول الشافعي، وفي بعض الفتاوى وتقبل شهادة الصديق لصديقه ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن المصرح به في غالب كتب أصحابنا والمشهور على ألسنة فقهائنا ما ذكره المصنف من التفصيل، ونقل في القنية أن العداوة بسبب الدنيا لا تمنع ما لم يفسق بسببها أو يجلب منفعة أو يدفع بها عن نفسه مضرة وهو الصحيح وعليه الاعتماد وما في الواقعات وغيرها اختيار المتأخرين، وأما الرواية المنصوصة فبخلافها. وفي كنز الرؤوس : شهادة العدو على عدوه لا تقبل لأنه متهم . وقال أبو حنيفة : تقبل إذا كان عدلاً. قال أستاذنا وهو الصحيح وعليه الاعتماد لأنه إذا كان عدلاً تقبل شهادته وإن كان بينهما عداوة بسبب أمر الدنيا ا هـ . واختاره ابن وهبان ولم يتعقبه ابن الشحنة لكن الحديث شاهد لما عليه المتأخرون كما رواه أبو داود مرفوعاً لا تجوز شهادة خائن ولا خائنة ولا زانٍ ولا زانية ولا ذي غمر على أخيه»(۱) والغمر الحقد ويمكن حمله على ما إذا كان غير عدل بدليل أن الحقد فسق للنهي عنه،
His saying: (and the two enemies if their enmity is worldly) i.e., the testimony of an enemy is not accepted due to worldly [reasons] because enmity for worldly matters is haram, so whoever commits it is not trustworthy... It is conditioned as worldly to exclude religious enmity, for it does not prevent [acceptance] because it indicates the perfection of his religion and uprightness. This is because enmity may be obligatory, such as when one sees a religious evil in another and he does not desist from his prohibition, as evidenced by the acceptance of a Muslim's testimony against a disbeliever despite the religious enmity between them, and the testimony of the murdered person's heir against the killer, and the wounded against the injurer, and the husband against his wife for adultery—mentioned by Ibn Wahban. ... And know that what is explicitly stated in most of our scholars' books and well-known among our jurists is what the author mentioned of detail, and it is transmitted in al-Qunyah that enmity due to worldly matters does not prevent [acceptance] as long as he does not become sinful because of it, or bring benefit, or ward off harm from himself, and this is sound and upon it reliance... And in Kanz al-Ru'us: the testimony of an enemy against his enemy is not accepted because he is accused. Abu Hanifa said: it is accepted if he is upright. Our teacher [said]: this is sound and upon it reliance... And Ibn Wahban chose this and Ibn al-Shihnah did not critique him, but the hadith supports the later scholars' view, as narrated by Abu Dawud, marfu': 'The testimony of a traitor, male or female, an adulterer, male or female, and one harboring rancor against his brother is not valid.' And al-ghimr means rancor, and it can be interpreted as when he is not upright, because rancor is fisq due to the prohibition against it.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exploration of the ruling on accepting testimony from an enemy. The matn states that testimony is rejected if the enmity is worldly. The commentator clarifies that religious enmity does not cause rejection, as it indicates righteousness. He provides examples: a Muslim testifying against a non-Muslim, an heir against the killer, etc. Then the text discusses the ikhtilaf: most Hanafi books detail that worldly enmity only prevents acceptance if it leads to fisq or personal benefit/detriment. Abu Hanifa is reported to accept testimony of an enemy if he is 'adl (upright), and this is relied upon by many later scholars. However, a hadith from Abu Dawud mentions rejecting testimony of one with rancor. The commentator reconciles by restricting the hadith to those who are not upright, since rancor is sinful. This presents a nuanced position: theoretically, enmity alone does not disqualify; but in practice, the hadith advises caution.

Key Terms
العدو (al-aduw): An enemy; defined as one who rejoices at your sorrow and grieves at your joy, or known by custom.
الحقد (al-hiqd): Rancor, a deep-seated enmity that is sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), when is the testimony of an enemy with worldly enmity rejected?
A) It is always rejected, regardless of the witness's character.
B) It is always accepted, because enmity does not affect testimony.
C) It is rejected only if the enmity leads the witness to commit fisq or brings him personal benefit/warding off harm.
D) It is rejected only if the enmity is religious.

Key Takeaway
The testimony of an enemy with worldly enmity is accepted in the Hanafi school if the witness remains upright and the enmity does not lead to fisq or personal interest; religious enmity does not disqualify.

Testimony of Two Accomplices: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Pardon in Qisas
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimonial capacity of co-defendants in qisas pardon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on testimony of two murderers regarding pardon of the third and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence of the dispute).

Core Idea
Two murderers testifying that the heir pardoned the third: accepted by Muhammad, rejected by Abu Yusuf, and partially by Abu Hanifa.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the admissibility of testimony by co-defendants in a homicide case. The core case from al-Muhit involves three murderers; two testify that the heir pardoned the third. The three founding imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad—disagree on whether such testimony is accepted and to what effect. We will walk through their positions, the reasoning behind each, and the practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Three men kill a man; two of them testify that the legal guardian (wali) has pardoned the third killer. The testimony is about the third, not themselves.
The witnesses are themselves co-murderers, so there is a clear potential benefit: avoiding qisas.
2
Identify each imam's position
From the passage: Muhammad accepts the testimony fully; Abu Yusuf rejects it entirely; Abu Hanifa (via the hashya) accepts it only to drop qisas from the two witnesses but they must pay diyah.
The hashya clarifies Abu Hanifa's view: 'تقبل في حق الواحد ويسقط القصاص عن الاثنين ويلزمهما بقية الدية'.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Muhammad argues no tuhmah (accusation) because the testimony is for the other, not for themselves. Abu Yusuf sees tuhmah because they benefit by saving the third (and thus themselves indirectly). Abu Hanifa sees partial benefit.
The later commentary argues that on Muhammad's view, it becomes an exception to the rule 'no testimony for oneself'.
4
Determine thamarah al-khilaf
If accepted (Muhammad): all three go free. If partially accepted (Abu Hanifa): the two witnesses avoid qisas but must pay the remaining diyah. If rejected (Abu Yusuf): all three are liable for qisas.
This practical difference shows the importance of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : : لا تقبل بدين له بعد موته وهنا مسائل أخرى : الأولى ثلاثة قتلوا رجلاً فشهد اثنان منهم على أن الولي عفا الثالث تقبل عند محمد لا عند أبي يوسف. عن الثانية ثلاثة عليهم دين شهد اثنان منهم على الدائن بإبراء الثالث فعلى الخلاف إن كانا لم يقبضا وإلا فلا اتفافاً. الثالثة شهد اثنان من الورثة على الباقي بأن هذا ابن الميت تقبل. الرابعة شهد الكفيلان بالعهدة على البائع بأنه قبض الثمن أو أبرأ المشتري منه لم تقبل كما في الخانية . مسألة الشهادة بالعفو لو شهدوا أنه عفا عنا قال الحسن : تقبل إذا قال اثنان في عفا عنا هذا الواحد فتقبل في حق الكل. وقال أبو يوسف: تقبل في حق الواحد في الخانية. ونظير هذه ما في الخانية أيضاً : لو قال إن دخل داري أحد فعبد حر فشهد ثلاثة أنهم دخلوها قال أبو يوسف : إن قالوا دخلناها جميعاً لا تقبل وإن قالوا دخلنا ودخل هذا تقبل. وسأل الحسن بن أبي يوسف عنها فقال : إن شهد ثلاثة بأنا دخلناها جميعاً تقبل، وإن شهد اثنان لا تقبل. فقال له الحسن: أصبت وخالفت أباك ا هـ .
وقد ذكر ابن وهبان رحمه الله تنبيهات حسنة لم أرها لغيره : الأول الذي يقتضيه كلام الحموي تبعاً للرملي : لا يصح استثناء هذه المسألة من الضابط المذكور لأنه ليس فيها قبول شهادة الإنسان لنفسه ولا على قول الحسن، بل إنما قبلت على قوله في الوجه المذكور لأنها شهادة الاثنين كل منهم على عفو الولي عن الثالث وأما شهادة كل لنفسه فلا قائل بها والوجه في ذلك أن شهادة الاثنين للآخر لا تهمة فيها لعدم الاشتراك لوجوب القتل على كل واحد منهم كملاً فلم تجر منفعة فهي كشهادة غريمين الغريمين فتأمل .اهـ. وفي حاشيتها للكفيري قال أبو حنيفة : تقبل في حق الواحد ويسقط القصاص عن الاثنين ويلزمهما بقية الدية، وذلك لأن الشهادة ليست لأنفسهما. وقال الحسن تقبل في حق الكل وذلك لما فيه من اعتبار أن كل اثنين تكون شهادتهما لغيرهما، وإذا فرض ذلك فتحصل الشهادة في المعنى لكل من الاثنين للآخر فتقبل شهادة الكل ..اهـ. نقله بعض الفضلاء. وعلى هذا التقرير يصح الاستثناء لأن فيه قبول شهادة الإنسان لنفسه فتأمل . (۱) رواه أبو داود...
And in al-Muhit: It is not accepted for a debt after his death. And here are other issues: First, three men killed a man, and two of them testified that the heir pardoned the third; it is accepted according to Muhammad, not according to Abu Yusuf. Second, three owe a debt; two of them testified to the creditor that the third has been discharged; it follows the same ikhtilaf if they have not collected, otherwise it is agreed upon. Third, two heirs testified against the remaining one that this is the son of the deceased; it is accepted. Fourth, two guarantors testified against the seller that he received the price or discharged the buyer; it is not accepted, as in al-Khaniyah. Issue of testimony regarding pardon: if they testify that he pardoned us, al-Hasan said: it is accepted if two say 'he pardoned us and this one', so it is accepted regarding all. And Abu Yusuf said: it is accepted regarding one in al-Khaniyah. And similar to this is what is in al-Khaniyah also: if one says: 'If anyone enters my house, he is free,' and three testify that they entered it, Abu Yusuf said: if they say 'we all entered', it is not accepted; if they say 'we entered and this one entered', it is accepted. And al-Hasan ibn Abi Yusuf asked him about it, and he said: 'If three testify that we all entered, it is accepted; if two, not accepted.' So al-Hasan said to him: 'You are correct and you have contradicted your father.' End. ... And Ibn Wahban, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned good points that I have not seen from others: First, what is required by the statement of al-Hamawi following al-Ramli: It is not correct to except this issue from the mentioned principle because there is no acceptance of a person's testimony for himself, nor according to al-Hasan; rather it is accepted according to his statement in the mentioned aspect because it is testimony of two, each of them regarding the heir's pardon of the third. And as for testimony of each for himself, no one says it. And the aspect is that the testimony of two for the other has no accusation because there is no sharing since the killing is incumbent on each of them fully, so no benefit accrues; it is like testimony of two creditors for the two creditors? Reflect. End. And in its hashya by al-Kufiri: Abu Hanifa said: it is accepted regarding one, and qisas is dropped from two, and they incur the remainder of the diyah, because the testimony is not for themselves. And al-Hasan said: accepted regarding all, because every two's testimony is for others, and if that is assumed, testimony effectively occurs for each of the two for the other, so all testimony is accepted. End. Transmitted by some virtuous people. And based on this explanation, the exception is valid because it involves acceptance of a person's testimony for himself; reflect. (1) Narrated by Abu Dawud...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This lesson presents a series of related cases (masa'il) from al-Muhit, focusing on testimony by parties with a potential conflict of interest. The first case is the most detailed: three men kill a man; two testify that the heir pardoned the third. Muhammad al-Shaybani accepts this testimony, while Abu Yusuf rejects it. The ikhtilaf extends to similar cases (debt, heirs, guarantors). The text then discusses a parallel case about a conditional manumission (if anyone enters the house, he is free). The super-commentary by Ibn Wahban and al-Kufiri refines the dispute: Abu Hanifa's position is that the testimony is accepted only to drop qisas from the two witnesses themselves, but not fully for the third? Actually, the hashya states: Abu Hanifa says accepted regarding one (the third?) and qisas is dropped from two, meaning the two witnesses are liable for diyah? The text is ambiguous. The key point is that the later scholars (al-Hamawi, al-Ramli, al-Kufiri) discuss whether this case is an exception to the principle that a person's testimony for himself is not accepted. They conclude that on Muhammad's view, it is an exception because the witnesses testify for themselves indirectly (their testimony helps themselves). This is a critical refinement: the sharh identifies a rule and the hashya tests an edge case, showing the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الشهادة بالعفو (al-shahadah bi al-afw): Testimony that the legal guardian (wali) has pardoned the killer, relevant in qisas cases.
التهمة (al-tuhmah): Accusation of bias; a ground for rejecting testimony when the witness stands to benefit or avoid harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The testimony is accepted fully, and qisas is dropped from all three
B) The testimony is rejected entirely
C) The testimony is accepted only to drop qisas from the two witnesses, but they must pay the remainder of the diyah
D) The testimony is accepted only if the witnesses were not present during the murder

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of testimony by co-defendants depends on the imam's assessment of tuhmah, leading to different practical outcomes in qisas and diyah.

The Exception Principle: Can One Testify Indirectly for Oneself?
Super-commentary analyzing whether the case is an exception to the rule against self-testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the hashya considers Muhammad's position an exception to the principle that a person cannot testify for oneself, and evaluate the reasoning of al-Kufiri and Ibn Wahban.

Core Idea
Muhammad's acceptance of testimony becomes an exception because the witnesses indirectly testify for themselves.

This lesson dives into the super-commentary on the main dispute. Ibn Wahban and al-Kufiri analyze whether the case of two murderers testifying on pardon is an exception to the well-established rule that a person's testimony for himself is not accepted. We will explore the reasoning that leads to this conclusion and the careful pondering (ta'ammul) that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The rule: 'لا تقبل شهادة الإنسان لنفسه' (a person's testimony for himself is not accepted). This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi testimony law.
The rule is not stated explicitly in the passage but is implied by the discussion.
2
State al-Hamawi and al-Ramli's claim
They argue that the case is NOT an exception because the witnesses do not testify for themselves; they testify for the third. So the rule does not apply.
This is the initial position: no exception needed.
3
Explain Ibn Wahban's refinement
Ibn Wahban objects: on Muhammad's view, each pair of witnesses ultimately testifies for the other, but since the testimony benefits all, it effectively becomes testimony for oneself. So it IS an exception.
The key point: the testimony of A and B for C benefits A and B indirectly.
4
Analyze al-Kufiri's hashya
Al-Kufiri reports Abu Hanifa's position and then notes that based on this explanation (Ibn Wahban's), the exception is valid because the testimony is accepted despite benefiting the witnesses.
The hashya concludes with 'فتأمل' (reflect), indicating the issue is not fully settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in al-Muhit: It is not accepted for a debt after his death. And here are other issues: First, three men killed a man, and two of them testified that the heir pardoned the third; it is accepted according to Muhammad, not according to Abu Yusuf. Key Terms
الضابط (al-dabit): The general principle or rule that governs a set of cases. Here, it refers to the rule 'a person cannot testify for himself'.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): An exception to a general rule. The hashya argues that Muhammad's position creates an exception to the dabit.

The Distinction Between Worldly and Religious Enmity in Testimony
Explains the rule on accepting enemy testimony with distinction between worldly and religious enmity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on enemy testimony with the distinction between worldly and religious enmity, and apply the condition of sin, benefit, or harm.

Core Idea
Worldly enmity does not bar testimony unless it leads to sin; religious enmity never bars because it shows integrity.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced ruling from Kitab al-Shahadat: when does enmity between parties invalidate testimony? The Hanafi school distinguishes between enmity arising from worldly causes and that arising from religious obligation. You will learn why the later Hanafi masters rely on a specific condition—that the enmity must cause sin, benefit, or harm—over the literal meaning of a hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of enmity
The text distinguishes 'adawah dunyawiyyah and 'adawah diniyyah. Note that religious enmity is actually obligatory and indicates uprightness.
2
Understand the Hanafi default position
Worldly enmity alone does not bar testimony. The default is acceptance if the witness is 'adl (upright).
3
Recognize the exception
Enmity becomes a bar when it leads to fisq (sin), or the witness seeks benefit or repels harm through it.
This is the mufta bihi view.
4
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith from Abu Dawud forbids testimony of one with ghamr (rancor). The Hanafis interpret this as referring to a non-upright person, not merely any enemy.
Dalil is zanni al-thubut wa al-dalala.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Al-Shafi'i absolutely prohibits enemy testimony. Understand the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات باب من تقبل شهادته ومن لا تقبل من التقول عليه. قيد بكونها دنيوية للاحتراز عما إذا كانت دينية فإنها لا تمنع لأنها تدل على كمال دينه وعدالته ، وهذا لأن المعاداة قد تكون واجبة بأن رأى فيه منكراً شرعاً ولم ينته بنهيه بدليل قبول شهادة المسلم على الكافر مع ما بينهما من العداوة الدينية، والمقتول وليه على القاتل، والمجروح على الجارح والزوج على امرأته بالزنا ذكره ابن وهبان. وفي خزانة المفتين والعدو من يفرح بحزنه ويحزن لفرحه وقيل يعرف بالعرف ا هـ. ومثال العداوة الدنيوية أن يشهد المقذوف على القاذف والمقطوع عليه الطريق على القاطع، وفي إدخال الزوج هنا نظر فقد صرحوا بقبول شهادته عليها بالزنا إلا إذا قذفها أولاً، وإنما المنع مطلقاً قول الشافعي، وفي بعض الفتاوى وتقبل شهادة الصديق لصديقه ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن المصرح به في غالب كتب أصحابنا والمشهور على ألسنة فقهائنا ما ذكره المصنف من التفصيل، ونقل في القنية أن العداوة بسبب الدنيا لا تمنع ما لم يفسق بسببها أو يجلب منفعة أو يدفع بها عن نفسه مضرة وهو الصحيح وعليه الاعتماد وما في الواقعات وغيرها اختيار المتأخرين، وأما الرواية المنصوصة فبخلافها. وفي كنز الرؤوس : شهادة العدو على عدوه لا تقبل لأنه متهم . وقال أبو حنيفة : تقبل إذا كان عدلاً. قال أستاذنا وهو الصحيح وعليه الاعتماد لأنه إذا كان عدلاً تقبل شهادته وإن كان بينهما عداوة بسبب أمر الدنيا ا هـ . واختاره ابن وهبان ولم يتعقبه ابن الشحنة لكن الحديث شاهد لما عليه المتأخرون كما رواه أبو داود مرفوعاً لا تجوز شهادة خائن ولا خائنة ولا زانٍ ولا زانية ولا ذي غمر على أخيه والغمر الحقد ويمكن حمله على ما إذا كان غير عدل بدليل أن الحقد فسق للنهي عنه، وقد ذكر ابن وهبان رحمه الله تنبيهات حسنة لم أرها لغيره : الأول
Book of Testimonies, Chapter on whose testimony is accepted and whose is not. [He said:] 'From surmising against him.' It is restricted by being worldly to avoid the case when it is religious, for it does not prevent because it indicates the perfection of his religion and uprightness. This is because enmity may be obligatory: if he saw in him a religious wrong and he did not desist by his prohibition, evidenced by accepting the testimony of a Muslim against a disbeliever despite religious enmity between them, and of the murdered person's guardian against the murderer, of the injured against the injurer, and of the husband against his wife for adultery—mentioned by Ibn Wahban. In Khizanat al-Muftin: The enemy is one who rejoices at your sorrow and grieves at your joy; it is said it is known by custom. Examples of worldly enmity: the falsely accused testifying against the false accuser, the robbery victim testifying against the robber. As for including the husband here, there is consideration: they explicitly accept his testimony against her for adultery unless he first slandered her. Absolute prohibition is the view of al-Shafi'i. In some fatwas: the testimony of a friend for his friend is accepted. Then know that what is explicitly stated in most of our companions' books and well-known on the tongues of our jurists is the detail mentioned by the author. It is transmitted in al-Qunyah that enmity due to worldly matters does not prevent as long as one does not become sinful because of it, or bring benefit, or repel harm from oneself—this is correct and relied upon. What is in al-Waqi'at and others is the choice of the later scholars. As for the transmitted narration, it is contrary. In Kanz al-Ru'us: The testimony of an enemy against his enemy is not accepted because he is suspect. Abu Hanifa said: It is accepted if he is upright. Our teacher said: This is correct and relied upon, because if he is upright, his testimony is accepted even if there is enmity between them due to worldly matters. Ibn Wahban chose this, and Ibn al-Shihnah did not comment, but the hadith supports the later scholars: as reported by Abu Dawud marfu'an: 'The testimony of a treacherous man and a treacherous woman, an adulterer and an adulteress, and one harboring rancor against his brother is not permissible.' Rancor (al-ghamr) means grudge. It can be interpreted as when he is not upright, because rancor is sin due to the prohibition. Ibn Wahban (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned excellent admonitions I have not seen elsewhere: the first...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the impact of enmity (عداوة) on the admissibility of testimony. The author begins by explaining the distinction between worldly enmity and religious enmity. Religious enmity does not bar testimony because it may be obligatory and indicates the witness's religious commitment. Examples include a Muslim testifying against a disbeliever, a guardian of a murdered person against the murderer, etc. Worldly enmity, however, is discussed in detail. The author notes that the prevalent position in the Hanafi school is that worldly enmity alone does not prevent testimony unless it leads to sin (فسق), benefit (جلب منفعة), or repelling harm (دفع مضرة). This is the correct and relied-upon view. Abu Hanifa is quoted as accepting an enemy's testimony if he is upright. The hadith from Abu Dawud about the testimony of one harboring rancor is interpreted to refer to a non-upright person. The passage then transitions to the excellent admonitions of Ibn Wahban, which will be discussed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
العداوة الدنيوية (al-‘adāwah al-dunyawiyyah): Enmity arising from worldly matters, not religious obligation.
العداوة الدينية (al-‘adāwah al-dīniyyah): Enmity due to religious reasons, such as enmity towards sinners or disbelievers.
الغمر (al-ghamr): Rancor or deep-seated grudge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever there is any enmity between the parties.
B) Only if the enmity is due to religious reasons.
C) When the enmity causes the witness to become sinful, or he brings benefit or repels harm through it.
D) It never bars testimony if the witness is upright.

Key Takeaway
Worldly enmity does not bar testimony unless it leads to sin; the hadith is interpreted to apply only to unrighteous witnesses.

Shahadat — Testimony

Three Admonitions on Enmity and Testimony
Three admonitions clarifying enmity, sin, and judicial decisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on testimony of an enemy, distinguish between the grounds of fisq and tuhmah, and apply the principle that fisq is indivisible to related cases.

Core Idea
Enmity bars testimony only when it causes fisq, but fisq is indivisible—once proven, it affects all dealings.

This passage presents three crucial clarifications (تنبيهات) by Ibn Wahban on enmity and testimony. These clarifications resolve apparent conflicts, address the nature of the bar (whether it is fisq or mere suspicion), and apply the principle that fisq cannot be partial. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying rules of testimony in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first admonition: the dispute over testimony among co-defendants
Identify the scenario where two defendants each testify about a third defendant's pardon. Note Abu Hanifa's view that only the third benefits, and al-Hasan's view that all three benefit. Understand the reasoning: each is fully liable, so no suspicion. Note that accepting al-Hasan's view would create an exception to the rule that one cannot testify for oneself.
This is a intra-Hanafi dispute, though al-Hasan is not a Hanafi imam; the author uses it for comparison.
2
Analyze the second admonition: claim of enmity and its effect on the claimant's uprightness
Understand that a mere claim of enmity does not automatically make the claimant a sinner; he must prove the enmity. The claim itself is an admission of sin only if the enmity involves sinful actions.
3
Evaluate the third admonition: validity of a judge's verdict based on enemy testimony
Determine whether the bar is fisq or a stronger cause (tuhmah). If it is fisq, the verdict is valid because a judge's ruling based on a sinner's testimony is executable. If it is a stronger cause, the verdict is invalid regarding the enemy but valid for others.
Later scholars incline toward the fisq-based view in this context.
4
Apply the principle of indivisibility of fisq to a nazir of multiple waqfs
Extend the rule that fisq is indivisible: if a nazir betrays one waqf, his fisq spreads, requiring his removal from all waqfs. This is confirmed by a fatwa of Abu al-Su'ud.
This qiyas shows the practical implications of the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول الذي يقتضيه كلام الحموي تبعاً للرملي : لا يصح استثناء هذه المسألة من الضابط المذكور لأنه ليس فيها قبول شهادة الإنسان لنفسه ولا على قول الحسن، بل إنما قبلت على قوله في الوجه المذكور لأنها شهادة الاثنين كل منهم على عفو الولي عن الثالث وأما شهادة كل لنفسه فلا قائل بها والوجه في ذلك أن شهادة الاثنين للآخر لا تهمة فيها لعدم الاشتراك لوجوب القتل على كل واحد منهم كملاً فلم تجر منفعة فهي كشهادة غريمين الغريمين فتأمل .اهـ. وفي حاشيتها للكفيري قال أبو حنيفة : تقبل في حق الواحد ويسقط القصاص عن الاثنين ويلزمهما بقية الدية، وذلك لأن الشهادة ليست لأنفسهما. وقال الحسن تقبل في حق الكل وذلك لما فيه من اعتبار أن كل اثنين تكون شهادتهما لغيرهما، وإذا فرض ذلك فتحصل الشهادة في المعنى لكل من الاثنين للآخر فتقبل شهادة الكل ..اهـ. نقله بعض الفضلاء. وعلى هذا التقرير يصح الاستثناء لأن فيه قبول شهادة الإنسان لنفسه فتأمل . (۱) رواه أبو داود في كتاب الأقضية باب .١٦ الترمذي في كتاب الشهادات باب ٢. ابن ماجه في كتاب الأحكام باب ۳۰ أحمد في مسنده (٢/ ٢٠٤، ٢٠٨). صاحب القنية والمبسوط أنا إذا قلنا إن العداوة قادحة في الشهادة تكون قادحة في حق جميع الناس لا في حق العدو فقط وهو الذي يقتضيه الفقه فإن الفسق لا يتجزأ حتى يكون فاسقاً في حق شخص عدلاً في حق آخر ا هـ . قلت : ولهذا لم يقل المؤلف على عدوه بل أطلقه . الثاني لو ادعى شخص عداوة آخر يكون مجرد دعواه اعترافاً منه بفسق نفسه ولا يكون ذلك قادحاً في عدالة المدعي أنه عدو ما لم يثبت المدعي أنه عدو له الثالث لو قضى القاضي بشهادة العدو على عدوه أو على غير عدّوه هل يصح أو لا؟ إن قلنا إن المانع من قبول الشهادة هو الفسق فيكون حينئذ صحيحاً نافذاً لأن القاضي إذا قضى بشهادة الفاسق نفذ قضاؤه ويصح، وإن قلنا إنه لمعنى آخر أقوى من الفسق لا يصح في حق العدو ويصح في حق غيره. وذكر ابن الكمال في إصلاح الإيضاح أن شهادة العدو لعدوه جائزة عكس شهادة الأصل لفرعه ا هـ . وهذا يدل على أنها إنما لم تقبل للتهمة لا للفسق . الرابع قد يتوهم بعض المتفقهة والشهود أن كل من خاصم شخصاً في حق وادعى عليه حقاً أنه يصير عدوه فيشهد بينهما بالعداوة وليس كذلك، بل العداوة إنما تثبت بنحو ما ذكرت . نعم لو خاصم الشخص آخر في حق لا تقبل شهادته عليه في ذلك الحق كالوكيل لا تقبل شهادته فيما هو وكيل فيه ونحو ذلك لا أنه إذا تخاصم اثنان في حق لا تقبل شهادة أحدهما على الآخر لما بينهما من المخاصمة ا هـ. قلت ويدل له ما في فتاوى قاضيخان من باب ما يبطل دعوى المدعي : قوله : (فإن الفسق لا يتجزأ (الخ وهل يقاس على هذا الناظر إذا كان عليه إنظار وقف عديدة وثبت فسقه بسبب خيانته في واحد منها، فهل يسري فسقه في كلها فيعزل؟ أقول: مقتضى قوله إن الفسق لا يتجزأ السريان فليتأمل وليراجع ثم رأيت ولله الحمد بعد مدة التصريح بذلك في فتاوى المفتي شيخ الإسلام أبي السعود العمادي المفسر ونصه في فتاويه من كتاب الوقف في ناظر على أوقاف متعددة ظهرت خيانته في بعض من الأوقاف هل يلزم عزله من الكل أو لا؟ الجواب لا بد ذلك ألبتة .اهـ. بحروفه . من كذا رأيته بخط منلاعلي أمين الفتوى بدمشق الشام في هامش نسخته. وكتب الرملي هنا الظاهر من كلامهم أن عدم القبول إنما هو للتهمة لا للفسق ويؤيده ما يأتي عن ابن الكمال وما صرح به یعقوب باشا، وكثير من علمائنا صرح ! بأن شهادة العدوّ على عدوّه لا تقبل فالتقييد بكونها على عدوّه ينفي ما عداه وهو المتبادر للأفهام فتأمله .اهـ. أقول : أنت خبير بأن فعل الكبيرة والإصرار على الصغيرة قادح في العدالة، وقد شرط في القنية لعدم القبول كونه فسق بتلك العداوة، وعلى هذا فعدم قبولها مطلقاً ظاهر، وينبغي تقييده بما إذا كانت عداوة ظاهرة كما يفيده ما يأتي عن الفتح في شرح قوله أو يرتكب ما : أن الوجه عدم القبول مطلقاً، والتعليل بالاتهام كما مر عن كنز الرؤوس لا ينافيه لأن الفاسق لا يقبل للاتهام أيضاً، وما يأتي عن ابن الكمال يمكن حمله على ما إذا لم يفسق بها فليتأمل قوله : (لأن القاضي إذا قضى بشهادة الفاسق نفذ قضاؤه ويصح) قال البحر الرائق ج۷/ م ١٠
The first [admonition]: What is required by the statement of al-Himawi following al-Ramli: It is not valid to except this case from the aforementioned rule, because it does not involve accepting a person's testimony for himself, nor according to the view of al-Hasan. Rather, it is accepted according to his view in the mentioned manner because it is the testimony of two, each of them regarding the guardian's pardon of the third. As for each testifying for himself, no one holds that. The reason is that the testimony of two for another has no suspicion because there is no sharing in the obligation of qisas—each is fully liable—so no benefit accrues. It is like the testimony of two creditors. So reflect. End quote. In its gloss by al-Kafiri: Abu Hanifa said: It is accepted regarding one, qisas is dropped from the two, and they are liable for the remaining diyah, because the testimony is not for themselves. Al-Hasan said: It is accepted regarding all, because each pair testifies for the other, so in effect each testifies for the other, thus all testimony is accepted. Some virtuous person transmitted this. According to this explanation, the exception is valid because it involves acceptance of a person's testimony for himself. So reflect. (1) Narrated by Abu Dawud in the Book of Judgments, chapter 16; al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Testimonies, chapter 2; Ibn Majah in the Book of Judgments, chapter 30; Ahmad in his Musnad (2/204, 208). The author of al-Qunyah and al-Mabsut: If we say that enmity is detrimental to testimony, it is detrimental regarding all people, not only the enemy. This is what fiqh requires, because fisq is indivisible: a person cannot be a sinner regarding one person and upright regarding another. End quote. I say: For this reason, the author did not say 'against his enemy' but said it absolutely. Second: If a person claims enmity of another, his mere claim is an admission of his own sin, and it does not affect the claimant's uprightness unless he proves that he is an enemy. Third: If a judge gives a verdict based on the testimony of an enemy against his enemy or against a non-enemy, is it valid or not? If we say the bar is fisq, then it is valid and executable because if a judge gives a verdict based on a sinner's testimony, it is valid and executed. If we say there is another meaning stronger than fisq, then it is not valid regarding the enemy but valid regarding others. Ibn al-Kamal mentioned in Islah al-Idah that testimony of an enemy for his enemy is permissible, contrary to testimony of a parent for his child. This indicates that it is not accepted due to suspicion (tuhmah), not fisq. Fourth: Some fuqaha and witnesses may mistakenly think that whoever litigates with someone over a right and claims a right becomes his enemy, and they claim enmity between them. That is not so. Enmity is established only as mentioned. However, if a person litigates with another over a right, his testimony against him in that right is not accepted, like an agent whose testimony is not accepted in matters he is an agent for, and the like. Not that when two litigate over a right, one's testimony against the other is not accepted due to the litigation between them. End quote. I say: This is supported by what is in Fatawa Qadikhan from the chapter on what invalidates a plaintiff's claim: his statement: 'Indeed, fisq is indivisible...' And is this analogized to a nazir who oversees multiple waqfs and his fisq is established due to betrayal in one of them? Does his fisq apply to all, thus removing him? I say: The implication of his statement that fisq is indivisible is that it spreads, so reflect and refer. Then after time I saw, praise be to Allah, the explicit statement in the fatwas of the mufti, Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Su'ud al-'Imadi al-Mufassir. Its text in his fatwas from the book of waqf: Regarding a nazir over multiple waqfs if his betrayal appears in some of the waqfs, is it necessary to remove him from all? The answer: Yes, absolutely. End quote verbatim. I saw it in the handwriting of the mufti of Damascus in the margin of his copy. Al-Ramli wrote: The apparent from their statements is that non-acceptance is due to suspicion (tuhmah) not fisq, supported by what came from Ibn al-Kamal and what Ya'qub Pasha stated. Many of our scholars explicitly stated that testimony of an enemy against his enemy is not accepted, so the restriction 'against his enemy' excludes others, which is what comes to mind. Reflect. End quote. I say: You are aware that committing a major sin and persisting in minor sin harms uprightness. It is stipulated in al-Qunyah that non-acceptance requires sin in that enmity. On this, non-acceptance absolutely is apparent, and it should be restricted to when enmity is apparent, as indicated by what comes from al-Fath in explaining his statement 'or commits that which necessitates hadd'. So it is determined. What comes from Ibn al-Kamal can be interpreted as when one does not sin by it, so reflect. His statement: 'Because if a judge gives a verdict based on the testimony of a sinner, it is valid and executable.' Al-Himawi said: Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, vol. 7, p. 10.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents three admonitions (تنبيهات) by Ibn Wahban, along with additional discussion. The first admonition addresses a specific scenario: two defendants each testify about the guardian's pardon of a third defendant. Abu Hanifa and Hasan al-Basri differ on acceptance. The author explains that if we accept the later view, it becomes an exception to the rule that one cannot testify for oneself. The second admonition clarifies that mere claim of enmity is an admission of sin in the claimant, but it does not affect his uprightness. The third admonition discusses whether a judge's verdict based on enemy testimony is valid. If the bar is fisq, the verdict is valid; if it is a stronger cause (like suspicion), it is invalid regarding the enemy. The text also includes a discussion on whether enmity affects all people or just the enemy, and a fatwa about a nazir of multiple waqfs: betrayal in one waqf leads to removal from all, because fisq is indivisible. The author concludes by reconciling views: non-acceptance is either due to suspicion or fisq, and the later scholars favor the latter. The entire passage is a rigorous analysis of the interplay between enmity, sin, and judicial decisions.

Key Terms
الفسق (al-fisq): Sin or transgression that harms a person's uprightness (عدالة).
التهمة (al-tuhmah): Suspicion of partiality or bias, which may bar testimony even without fisq.
الأصل والفرع (al-asl wa al-far'): Parent and child; testimony of parent for child is not accepted due to tuhmah.
العداوة (al-'adawah): Enmity between individuals; a potential bar to testimony if it leads to fisq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third admonition, what determines whether a judge's verdict based on enemy testimony is valid?
A) It is always invalid.
B) It is always valid.
C) If the bar is fisq, the verdict is valid; if it is a stronger cause like suspicion, it is invalid regarding the enemy.
D) It depends on whether the testimony was for or against the enemy.

Key Takeaway
Enmity bars testimony only through proven fisq, and fisq is indivisible, so its effects apply universally.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Testimony of an Enemy: Fisq, Suspicion, and Indivisibility of Moral Corruption
Ruling on the effect of enmity on witness testimony with proofs and subsidiary cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of an enemy, explain why fisq is indivisible, and distinguish between rejection due to fisq and rejection due to suspicion.

Core Idea
Fisq is indivisible: an enemy witness is disqualified universally if corrupt, but mere litigation does not create permanent enmity.

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between the rejection of a witness due to proven moral corruption (fisq) and rejection due to mere suspicion (tuhma) of bias from enmity. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the principle that fisq is indivisible—a person cannot be just for one party and sinful for another—and applies it to the testimony of an enemy. The commentary also addresses whether a judge's own enmity affects his rulings and extends the principle to waqf overseers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Ruling
State the general rule: the testimony of an enemy is not automatically rejected; it depends on whether enmity has led to fisq or creates a reasonable suspicion of bias.
The text opens by citing al-Qunyah and al-Mabsut that enmity, if disqualifying, disqualifies universally because fisq is indivisible.
2
Analyze the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is the established maxim 'al-fisq la yatajazza' (fisq is indivisible), supported by ijma. Trace how this principle leads to the ruling that an enemy witness, if fasiq, is rejected in all cases.
The maxim is based on rational deduction (dalil aqli) and consensus.
3
Examine the Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
Ibn al-Kamal argues that rejection is due to suspicion (tuhma) rather than fisq. Understand the difference: if due to tuhma, the testimony may still be accepted if the enmity is not proven to cause bias; if due to fisq, absolute rejection.
This affects whether a judge's ruling based on such testimony is valid (if fisq, valid; if suspicion, not valid against the enemy).
4
Apply to Subsidiary Cases
Work through the text's subsidiary rulings: (1) a claim of enmity is an admission of the claimant's own fisq; (2) mere litigation does not establish permanent enmity; (3) a waqf overseer's corruption extends to all waqfs; (4) a judge's personal enmity invalidates judgments based on his own knowledge but not those based on open court testimony.
These applications illustrate the practical scope of the principle.

كتاب الشهادات باب من تقبل شهادته ومن لا تقبل الناس صاحب القنية والمبسوط أنا إذا قلنا إن العداوة قادحة في الشهادة تكون قادحة في حق جميع لا في حق العدو فقط وهو الذي يقتضيه الفقه فإن الفسق لا يتجزأ حتى يكون فاسقاً في حق شخص عدلاً في حق آخر ا هـ . قلت : ولهذا لم يقل المؤلف على عدوه بل أطلقه . الثاني لو ادعى شخص عداوة آخر يكون مجرد دعواه اعترافاً منه بفسق نفسه ولا يكون ذلك قادحاً في عدالة المدعي أنه عدو ما لم يثبت المدعي أنه عدو له الثالث لو قضى القاضي بشهادة العدو على عدوه أو على غير عدّوه هل يصح أو لا؟ إن قلنا إن المانع من قبول الشهادة هو الفسق فيكون حينئذ صحيحاً نافذاً لأن القاضي إذا قضى بشهادة الفاسق نفذ قضاؤه ويصح، وإن قلنا إنه لمعنى آخر أقوى من الفسق لا يصح في حق العدو ويصح في حق غيره. وذكر ابن الكمال في إصلاح الإيضاح أن شهادة العدو لعدوه جائزة عكس شهادة الأصل لفرعه ا هـ . وهذا يدل على أنها إنما لم تقبل للتهمة لا للفسق . الرابع قد يتوهم بعض المتفقهة والشهود أن كل من خاصم شخصاً في حق وادعى عليه حقاً أنه يصير عدوه فيشهد بينهما بالعداوة وليس كذلك، بل العداوة إنما تثبت بنحو ما ذكرت . نعم لو خاصم الشخص آخر في حق لا تقبل شهادته عليه في ذلك الحق كالوكيل لا تقبل شهادته فيما هو وكيل فيه ونحو ذلك لا أنه إذا تخاصم اثنان في حق لا تقبل شهادة أحدهما على الآخر لما بينهما من المخاصمة ا هـ. قلت ويدل له ما في فتاوى قاضيخان من باب ما يبطل دعوى المدعي : قوله : (فإن الفسق لا يتجزأ (الخ وهل يقاس على هذا الناظر إذا كان عليه إنظار وقف عديدة وثبت فسقه بسبب خيانته في واحد منها، فهل يسري فسقه في كلها فيعزل؟ أقول: مقتضى قوله إن الفسق لا يتجزأ السريان فليتأمل وليراجع ثم رأيت ولله الحمد بعد مدة التصريح بذلك في فتاوى المفتي شيخ الإسلام أبي السعود العمادي المفسر ونصه في فتاويه من كتاب الوقف في ناظر على أوقاف متعددة ظهرت خيانته في بعض من الأوقاف هل يلزم عزله من الكل أو لا؟ الجواب لا بد ذلك ألبتة .اهـ. بحروفه . من كذا رأيته بخط منلاعلي أمين الفتوى بدمشق الشام في هامش نسخته. وكتب الرملي هنا الظاهر من كلامهم أن عدم القبول إنما هو للتهمة لا للفسق ويؤيده ما يأتي عن ابن الكمال وما صرح به یعقوب باشا، وكثير من علمائنا صرح ! بأن شهادة العدوّ على عدوّه لا تقبل فالتقييد بكونها على عدوّه ينفي ما عداه وهو المتبادر للأفهام فتأمله .اهـ. أقول : أنت خبير بأن فعل الكبيرة والإصرار على الصغيرة قادح في العدالة، وقد شرط في القنية لعدم القبول كونه فسق بتلك العداوة، وعلى هذا فعدم قبولها مطلقاً ظاهر، وينبغي تقييده بما إذا كانت عداوة ظاهرة كما يفيده ما يأتي عن الفتح في شرح قوله أو يرتكب ما : أن الوجه عدم القبول مطلقاً، والتعليل بالاتهام كما مر عن كنز الرؤوس لا ينافيه لأن الفاسق لا يقبل للاتهام أيضاً، وما يأتي عن ابن الكمال يمكن حمله على ما إذا لم يفسق بها فليتأمل قوله : (لأن القاضي إذا قضى بشهادة الفاسق نفذ قضاؤه ويصح) قال البحر الرائق ج۷/ م ١٠ وممدن الشرب على اللهو ومن يلعب بالطنبور أو يغني للناس أو يرتكب ما يوجب У يمتنع رجل خاصم رجلاً في دار أو في حق ثم إن هذا الرجل شهد عليه في حق آخر جازت شهادته إذا كان عدلاً ا هـ . واعلم أنه لو شهد على رجل آخر فخاصمه في شيء قبل القضاء القضاء بشهادته إلا إذا ادعى أنه دفع له كذا لئلا يشهد عليه وطلب الرد وأثبت دعواه ببينة أو إقرار أو نكول فحينئذ بطلت شهادته وهو جرح مقبول كما صرحوا به وسيأتي في بيان الجرح. الخامس إذا قلنا لا تجوز شهادة العدو على عدوه إذا كانت دنيوية هل الحكم في القاضي كذلك حتى لا يجوز قضاء القاضي على من بينه وبينه عداوة دنيوية؟ لم أقف عليه في كتب أصحابنا وينبغي أن يكون الجواب فيه على التفصيل؛ إن كان قضاؤه عليه بعلمه ينبغي أن لا ينفذ وإن كان بشهادة العدول وبمحضر من الناس في مجلس الحكم بطلب خصم شرعي ينبغي أن ينفذ وفرق الماوردي من الشافعية بينهما بأن أسباب الحكم ظاهرة وأسباب الشهادة خافية ا هـ.
Book of Testimonies, Chapter on whose testimony is accepted and whose is not. The people [said]: The author of al-Qunyah and al-Mabsut said: If we say that enmity is detrimental in testimony, it is detrimental regarding all people, not only regarding the enemy. And this is what fiqh requires, because fisq is indivisible, such that a person cannot be a sinner regarding one person and just regarding another. End quote. I say: That is why the author did not say 'against his enemy' but spoke generally. Second: If a person claims enmity against another, his mere claim is an admission of his own fisq, and it does not detract from the claimant's justice that he is an enemy, unless the claimant proves that he is an enemy. Third: If a judge passes a judgment based on the testimony of an enemy against his enemy or against someone else, is it valid or not? If we say that the preventer of acceptance is fisq, then it is valid and effective because if a judge passes judgment based on the testimony of a sinful person, his judgment is executed and is valid. If we say that it is due to another meaning stronger than fisq, then it is not valid regarding the enemy but is valid regarding others. Ibn al-Kamal mentioned in Islah al-Idah that the testimony of an enemy on behalf of his enemy is permissible, opposite to the testimony of an originator for his branch. End quote. This indicates that it is not accepted due to suspicion, not due to fisq. Fourth: Some students and witnesses might mistakenly think that whoever disputes with a person over a right and claims a right against him becomes his enemy, and so they testify between them about enmity. But it is not like that; rather, enmity is only established by what was mentioned. Yes, if a person disputes with another over a right, his testimony against him regarding that right is not accepted, like an agent whose testimony regarding what he is an agent for is not accepted, and so on. It does not mean that if two people dispute over a right, the testimony of one against the other is not accepted due to the dispute between them. End quote. I say: This is supported by what is in Fatawa Qadikhan from the chapter on what invalidates the plaintiff's claim: His statement: (For fisq is indivisible) etc. Should this be analogized to a overseer of multiple waqfs whose fisq is proven due to betrayal in one of them? Does his fisq extend to all, removing him? I say: The requirement of his statement that fisq is indivisible implies extension, so let it be considered and referred. Then I saw, praise be to God, after a period, the explicit statement of that in the Fatawa of the mufti, Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Su'ud al-Imadi al-Mufassir. His text in his Fatawa, from the book of waqf, concerning an overseer of multiple waqfs whose betrayal appeared in some of the waqfs: Is his removal from all necessary or not? The answer: It is absolutely necessary. End quote, letter for letter. Thus I saw it in the handwriting of the one who put it on the margin of his copy from the official fatwa in Damascus. Al-Ramli wrote here: The apparent from their speech is that non-acceptance is due to suspicion, not fisq, and it is supported by what comes from Ibn al-Kamal and what Ya'qub Pasha explicitly stated. Many of our scholars explicitly stated that the testimony of an enemy against his enemy is not accepted, so the restriction to being against his enemy negates what is other than it, and it is what is immediately understood. So contemplate it. End quote. I say: You are aware that committing a major sin and persisting in a minor sin is detrimental to justice. Al-Qunyah stipulated that for non-acceptance, the enmity must involve fisq. Accordingly, absolute non-acceptance is apparent, and it should be restricted to when the enmity is apparent, as is indicated by what comes from al-Fath in his commentary on his statement: 'or he commits what requires hadd.' So it is established. That the correct view is absolute non-acceptance, and the reasoning by suspicion as mentioned in Kanz al-Ru'us does not contradict it because the sinful person is also not accepted due to suspicion. And what comes from Ibn al-Kamal can be interpreted as referring to when he is not sinful due to it. So contemplate. His statement: (Because if the judge passes judgment based on the testimony of a sinful person, his judgment is executed and is valid.) He said: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol. 7, part 10. And one addicted to drinking for amusement and one who plays the tambourine or sings to the people or commits what requires [had] is prevented. A man disputed with a man over a house or a right, then this man testified against him in another right, his testimony is permissible if he is just. End quote. And know that if he testifies against another man and then disputes with him in something before the judgment, his testimony is not accepted unless he claims that he paid him such-and-such so that he would not testify against him, and he requests rejection and proves his claim by evidence, admission, or refusal to take an oath; then his testimony becomes invalid and it is an accepted discreditation, as they have explicitly stated, and it will come in the explanation of discreditation. Fifth: If we say that the testimony of an enemy against his enemy is not permissible when it is worldly, is the ruling regarding the judge also such that the judge may not pass judgment against someone with whom he has worldly enmity? I did not come across it in the books of our scholars. It should be answered in detail: If his judgment is based on his own knowledge, it should not be executed. If it is based on the testimony of just witnesses and in the presence of people in the court of law upon the request of a legal opponent, it should be executed. Al-Mawardi from the Shafi'is distinguished between them by saying that the causes of judgment are apparent while the causes of testimony are hidden. End quote.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the effect of enmity (عداوة) on the acceptance of testimony. The author begins by citing al-Qunyah and al-Mabsut that if enmity is a disqualifying factor, it disqualifies the witness universally, not just regarding the enemy, because moral corruption (فسق) is indivisible: a person cannot be sinful in one context and righteous in another. This is the basis of the Hanafi position. The author then mentions a series of subsidiary rulings (فروع): First, a claim of enmity by one person against another is an admission of the claimant's own fisq, but it does not disqualify him as a witness unless he proves the enmity. Second, if a judge accepts testimony from an enemy and passes judgment, the validity depends on the reason for disqualification: if it is fisq, the judgment is valid because a judge's ruling on the testimony of a sinful person stands; if it is a stronger reason like suspicion, the judgment is invalid regarding the enemy. Ibn al-Kamal's statement that testimony of an enemy for his enemy is permissible suggests the barrier is suspicion, not fisq. Third, mere litigation does not establish enmity for all future testimony; it only disqualifies for that specific case, analogous to an agent's testimony. The author supports this with a fatwa from Qadikhan. Fourth, the commentary addresses whether the principle that fisq is indivisible extends to a waqf overseer who is corrupt in one waqf—should he be removed from all? The author cites a fatwa from Abu al-Su'ud affirming this. Fifth, the author discusses the judge's own enmity: if the judge has personal enmity, his judgment based on his own knowledge is invalid, but if based on witness testimony in open court, it stands. This is from al-Mawardi. Throughout, the Hanafi position is that outward bias (ظاهر) is key, and suspicion is the primary reason for rejecting an enemy's testimony, not necessarily fisq.

Key Terms
عداوة (adāwah): Enmity that may disqualify a witness if it is proven to be animosity, not mere litigation.
فسق (fisq): Moral corruption, which is indivisible; a witness known to be fasiq is rejected in all matters.
تهمة (tuhmah): Suspicion of bias or interest, which can be a reason to reject testimony even without fisq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as discussed in this passage, what is the primary reason for rejecting the testimony of an enemy against his enemy?
A) The enemy status itself, regardless of fisq
B) The suspicion of bias (tuhmah) rather than necessarily fisq
C) The enemy must have committed a major sin against the witness
D) The judge's personal opinion

Key Takeaway
An enemy's testimony is not automatically invalid; it depends on whether enmity has caused fisq or creates a reasonable suspicion of bias.
