Conditions for Rejecting Testimony of an Addictive Drinker
Explains conditions for accepting testimony of someone addicted to drinking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for rejecting the testimony of one addicted to intoxicating drinks, and distinguish the rulings for wine versus other intoxicants.

Core Idea
Testimony of a wine drinker is rejected upon addiction; for other intoxicants, intoxication and amusement are required.

This passage unpacks the matn phrase 'one addicted to drinking for amusement'. We explore when habitual consumption of prohibited drinks invalidates testimony, revealing a nuanced Hanafi position that distinguishes wine from other intoxicants and requires public knowledge or intention to continue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the testimony of a person who habitually drinks prohibited substances. The ruling: such testimony is not accepted (la tuqbal).
2
Locate the evidence
The text cites later Hanafi authorities (al-Khaniyyah, al-Dhakhirah) rather than primary revealed sources, indicating that the ruling is based on consensus of scholars regarding 'adala and fisq.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Zayla'i requires addiction and 'for amusement' even for wine. Mulla Khusraw and author of al-Bahr hold that for wine, the condition of amusement is not needed because drinking wine is a major sin in itself.
The dispute centers on whether the matn's phrase 'shurb ala al-lahw' applies to all prohibited drinks or only non-wine ones.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator sides with the author of al-Bahr: for wine, addiction alone (without amusement) suffices to reject testimony; for other intoxicants, addiction to intoxication with amusement is required.
This is the operative position for fatwa.
5
Apply to a case
Example: A witness drinks wine occasionally but never publicly. His testimony may still be accepted if he is not an addict (i.e., does not intend to continue). A witness who regularly drinks non-wine intoxicants for amusement and becomes drunk is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومدمن الشرب على اللهو ) أي لا تقبل شهادة المداوم على شرب ما لا يحل شربه فأطلق اللهو على المشروب، وظاهره أنه لا بد من الإدمان في حق الخمر أيضاً. وفي الخانية : إنما شرط الإدمان ليظهر ذلك عند الناس فإن من اتهم بشرب الخمر في بيته لا تبطل عدالته وإن كانت كبيرة، وإنما تبطل إذا ظهر ذلك أو يخرج سكران يسخر منه الصبيان لأن مثله لا يحترز عن الكذب واختاره المصنف في الكافي وفي النهاية معزياً إلى الذخيرة: لا يجوز شهادة مدمن الخمر ثم قال : بشرط الإدمان ولم يرد به الإدمان في الشرب وإنما أراد به الإدمان في النية يعني يشرب ومن نيته أن يشرب بعد ذلك إذا وجده ولا تجوز شهادة مدمن السكر وأراد به السكر بسائر الأشربة سوى الخمر لأن المحرم في سائر الأشربة السكر فشرط الرملي وصرح يعقوب باشا في حاشيته بعدم نفاذ قضاء القاضي بشهادة العدوّ على عدوّه. وأقول : وقياسه يقتضي أن العصبية كذلك فلا ينفذ قضاء القاضي بشهادته لأنه الذي يبغض الرجل لكونه من فلان أو من قبيلة كذا كما سيأتي في الحاشية قريباً منقولاً عن معين الحكم فتأمله . بني قوله: (فأطلق اللهو على المشروب الخ) قال في المنح : هو خلاف الظاهر من العبارة لأن الظاهر منها أن معنى مدمن الشرب أي مداوم شرب الخمر على اللهو وقال الزيلعي أي مداوم شرب الخمر لأجل اللهو لأن شربها كبيرة وقال منلا خسرو ومدمن الشرب أي شرب الأشربة المحرمة فإن إدمان شرب غيرها لا يسقط الشهادة ما لم يكن على اللهو .اهـ. فأفاد كلامه أن الشرب على اللهو إنما هو شرط في غير الأشربة المحرمة أما فيها فلا يشترط وهذا يوافق كلام صاحب البحر. والظاهر أن هذا هو الذي أحوجه إلى ما ذكره من حمل اللهو في كلام الكنز على المشروب مخالف لكلام الزيلعي فإنه جعله شرطاً في الخمر أيضاً والظاهر خلافه لأن شرب الخمر كبيرة ترد الشهادة بها، سواء شربت على اللهو أم لا، وظاهر كلامهم أنه لا بد من الإدمان في حق الخمر وهو
His statement: (And one addicted to drinking for amusement) i.e., the testimony of one who continues to drink what is not permissible to drink is not accepted. So he used 'amusement' for the drink. Apparently, addiction is necessary regarding wine as well. In al-Khaniyyah: Addiction is only stipulated so that it becomes apparent among people, because one who is accused of drinking wine in his home, his justice is not invalidated even though it is a major sin. It only becomes invalid when it becomes apparent or he comes out drunk such that children mock him, because the like of him does not guard against lying. Al-Musannaf chose this in al-Kafi, and in al-Nihayah, citing al-Dhakhirah: The testimony of a wine addict is not permissible. Then he said: With the condition of addiction, but he did not mean addiction in drinking; rather, he meant addiction in intention, meaning he drinks with the intention to drink afterward if he finds it. And the testimony of one addicted to intoxication is not permissible, meaning intoxication from other drinks besides wine, because in other drinks the prohibition is intoxication. So he stipulated. Al-Ramli and Ya'qub Pasha explicitly stated in his marginal note that the rule of the judge based on the testimony of an enemy against his enemy is not executed. And I say: Analogy requires that partisanship is similar, so the judge's judgment based on his testimony is not executed because it is due to hatred of a person for being from such-and-such family or tribe, as will come in the margin soon, quoted from Mu'in al-Hukm. So contemplate. Regarding his statement: (So he used 'amusement' for the drink etc.), al-Minnah said: This is contrary to the apparent meaning of the expression, because the apparent meaning is that 'addicted to drinking' means one who continues to drink wine for amusement. Al-Zayla'i said: One who continues to drink wine for amusement, because drinking it is a major sin. Mulla Khusraw said: 'Addicted to drinking' means drinking prohibited drinks, because addiction to other drinks does not remove testimony unless it is for amusement. End quote. So his statement indicates that drinking for amusement is only a condition for non-prohibited drinks; as for prohibited ones, it is not required. This agrees with the statement of the author of al-Bahr. And apparently this is what necessitated his interpretation of 'amusement' in Kanz as the drink, contrary to al-Zayla'i, who made it a condition regarding wine as well. The apparent is contrary, because drinking wine is a major sin that rejects testimony, whether drunk for amusement or not. And apparently their statement requires addiction regarding wine. And it is...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'ومدمن الشرب على اللهو' (one addicted to drinking for amusement). The commentator explains that this refers to someone who habitually drinks whatever is prohibited, and the word 'لهو' (amusement) is used metaphorically for the drink itself. The key issue is whether addiction (إدمان) is a necessary condition for rejecting testimony. According to al-Khaniyyah, addiction is required not for the sin itself but for public knowledge: if a person drinks wine secretly, his justice is not impaired until it becomes known or he appears drunk publicly. The author of al-Kafi and al-Nihayah hold that the testimony of a wine addict is not permissible, but addiction here means intention to continue, not continuous drinking. For drinks other than wine, the prohibition is intoxication, so only one addicted to intoxication is rejected. The commentator then notes that al-Ramli and Ya'qub Pasha applied this to enmity cases, but that is a separate issue. The commentator also discusses the disagreement among Hanafi scholars: al-Zayla'i requires the condition of 'for amusement' even for wine, while Mulla Khusraw and the author of al-Bahr hold that for wine, amusement is not a condition because it is a major sin regardless. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that for wine, addiction and drunkenness are not required; mere drinking of wine, even once, technically makes one a fasiq, but the commentary notes that for testimony rejection, addiction is needed to establish a pattern of fisq (though the text does not explicitly resolve this). Praactically, Shafi'is may reject immediately on one instance of wine drinking, while Hanafis require continuous conduct or public notoriety.

Key Terms
إدمان (idmān): Habitual or continuous consumption, or the intention to continue drinking when possible.
شرب على اللهو (shurb ʿalā al-lahw): Drinking for amusement, which is a condition for rejecting testimony from one who drinks non-wine intoxicants.
سكر (sukr): Intoxication, which is the prohibited aspect in drinks other than wine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentary in this passage, when does drinking a non-wine intoxicating drink disqualify a witness?
A) Immediately upon the first drink, regardless of intoxication
B) Only if the person is addicted to drinking for amusement and becomes intoxicated
C) Only if the person drinks openly in public
D) Never, because non-wine drinks are not prohibited

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position requires addiction or public notoriety for rejecting testimony of a drinker; wine is treated specially due to its inherent prohibition.

Testimony of the Habitual Drinker: Addiction and 'Adala
Expansion-probe commentary on the habitual drinker's testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a habitual drinker and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the role of addiction (idman) in rejecting testimony.

Core Idea
Wine drinking is a major sin that immediately nullifies 'adala; addiction is only for judicial knowledge, not substantive.

The testimony of a witness is accepted only if he is 'adil (morally upright). But what if he drinks wine or other intoxicants? Does a single sip nullify his 'adala, or must he be addicted? The Hanafi school has an important internal dispute on this question, rooted in the classification of wine drinking as a major vs. minor sin and the role of apparent sin in legal proceedings. This lesson unpacks the passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that clarifies the ruling and the reasoning of the imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Understand that the question is whether addiction (idman) to drinking wine or other intoxicants is required for the testimony to be rejected. The passage distinguishes between wine and other intoxicants.
The phrase 'madman al-shurb' (habitual drinker) in the matn is ambiguous.
2
Locate the primary ruling
The text states that the testimony of a habitual drinker is not accepted. But the commentary explores whether 'habitual' means addicted, and for which drinks.
For wine, the relied-upon view: no addiction needed.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Khassaf held that wine drinking even once invalidates 'adala because it is a major sin. Imam Muhammad required addiction (idman) for invalidation.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The proof cited is the hadith listing wine as a major sin. Since major sins nullify 'adala immediately, addiction is not required.
Hanafi usul: a major sin (kabira) destroys 'adala without persistence.
5
Apply the distinction between wine and other intoxicants
For other intoxicants (e.g., nabidh), addiction is necessary because the prohibited act is intoxication itself; drinking a non-intoxicating amount is a minor sin and only persistence makes it major.
This explains why the text specifies 'on amusement' for non-wine drinks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات باب من تقبل شهادته ومن لا تقبل
ومدمن الشرب على اللهو ومن يلعب بالطنبور أو يغني للناس أو يرتكب ما يوجب
رجل خاصم رجلاً في دار أو في حق ثم إن هذا الرجل شهد عليه في حق آخر جازت شهادته إذا كان عدلاً ا هـ . واعلم أنه لو شهد على رجل آخر فخاصمه في شيء قبل القضاء القضاء بشهادته إلا إذا ادعى أنه دفع له كذا لئلا يشهد عليه وطلب الرد وأثبت دعواه ببينة أو إقرار أو نكول فحينئذ بطلت شهادته وهو جرح مقبول كما صرحوا به وسيأتي في بيان الجرح. الخامس إذا قلنا لا تجوز شهادة العدو على عدوه إذا كانت دنيوية هل الحكم في القاضي كذلك حتى لا يجوز قضاء القاضي على من بينه وبينه عداوة دنيوية؟ لم أقف عليه في كتب أصحابنا وينبغي أن يكون الجواب فيه على التفصيل؛ إن كان قضاؤه عليه بعلمه ينبغي أن لا ينفذ وإن كان بشهادة العدول وبمحضر من الناس في مجلس الحكم بطلب خصم شرعي ينبغي أن ينفذ وفرق الماوردي من الشافعية بينهما بأن أسباب الحكم ظاهرة وأسباب الشهادة خافية ا هـ.
قوله : ( ومدمن الشرب على اللهو أي لا تقبل شهادة المداوم على شرب ما لا يحل شربه فأطلق اللهو على المشروب، وظاهره أنه لا بد من الإدمان في حق الخمر أيضاً. وفي الخانية : إنما شرط الإدمان ليظهر ذلك عند الناس فإن من اتهم بشرب الخمر في بيته لا تبطل عدالته وإن كانت كبيرة، وإنما تبطل إذا ظهر ذلك أو يخرج سكران يسخر منه الصبيان لأن مثله لا يحترز عن الكذب واختاره المصنف في الكافي وفي النهاية معزياً إلى الذخيرة: لا يجوز شهادة مدمن الخمر ثم قال : بشرط الإدمان ولم يرد به الإدمان في الشرب وإنما أراد به الإدمان في النية يعني يشرب ومن نيته أن يشرب بعد ذلك إذا وجده ولا تجوز شهادة مدمن السكر وأراد به السكر بسائر الأشربة سوى الخمر لأن المحرم في سائر الأشربة السكر فشرط
الرملي وصرح يعقوب باشا في حاشيته بعدم نفاذ قضاء القاضي بشهادة العدوّ على عدوّه. وأقول : وقياسه يقتضي أن العصبية كذلك فلا ينفذ قضاء القاضي بشهادته لأنه الذي يبغض الرجل لكونه من فلان أو من قبيلة كذا كما سيأتي في الحاشية قريباً منقولاً عن معين الحكم فتأمله .

سبع
الإدمان على السكر والمحرم في الخمر نفس الشرب فشرط الإدمان على الشرب ا هـ . والتحقيق خلاف كل من القولين وأن الإدمان بالفعل أو النية ليش بشرط في الخمر لأن شرب قطرة كبيرة منها وهي مسقطة للعدالة من غير إصرار، وإنما ذكر المشايخ الإدمان ليظهر شربه عند القاضي لا أنه شرط كقولهم إن النائحة لا تسقط عدالتها إلا إذا كانت نائحة في مصيبة غيرها مع أن النياحة كبيرة للتوعد عليها لكن لا يظهر إلا في مصيبة غيرها غالباً. وأما في غير الخمر فلا بد من الإدمان لأن شربه صغيرة والقولان في تفسير الإدمان محكيان في تفسير الإصرار عليها. وذكر ابن الكمال أن شرب الخمر ليس بكبيرة فلا تسقط العدالة إلا بالإصرار عليه. قال في الفتاوى الصغرى ولا تسقط عدالة شارب الخمر بنفس الشرب لأن غلط من ابن الكمال لما قدمناه هذا الحد لم يثبت بنص قاطع إلا إذا داوم على ذلك اهـ. وهو عن المشايخ من التصريح بأن شربها كبيرة ولمخالفته للحديث المشهور في الكبائر أنها وذكر منها شرب الخمر، وليس في كلام الصغرى أنها صغيرة كما لا يخفى لكن في تعليله نظر لأن الكلام فيها لا في الحد وحرمتها تثبتت بدلیل مقطوع به ولذا قالوا: يكفر مستحلها . وسقوط العدالة إنما هو بسبب شربها لا بسبب وجوب الحد عليه. وذكر الصدر الشهيد في شرح أدب القضاء إن الخصاف أسقط العدالة بشرب الخمر من غير إدمان ومحمد شرط الإدمان لسقوطها وهو الصحيح ا.هـ. وفي العتابية لا تسقط عدالة أصحاب المروءات بالشرب ما لم يشتهر . وفي الظهيرية من سكر من النبيذ بطلت عدالته في قول الخصاف لأن السكر حرام عند الكل. وقال محمد : لا تبطل عدالته إلا إن اعتاد ذلك اهـ. وهو عجيب من محمد لأنه قال بحرمة قليله ولم يسقطها بكثيره، وظاهره أنه يقول بأن السكر منه صغيرة فشرط الاعتياد. فإن قلت : هل لشارب الخمر أن يشهد إذا لم يطلع عليه؟ قلت : نعم لما في الملتقط : وإذا كان في الظاهر عدلاً وفي السر فاسقاً فأراد القاضي أن يقضي بشهادته لا يحل له أن يذكر فسقه لأنه هتك السر وإبطال حق المدعي ا هـ . ولا فرق في السكر المسقط لها
أيضاً اهـ. قوله : (والتحقيق خلاف كل من القولين قال الرملي في حاشية المنح: لا يخفى حسن ما في النهاية معزواً إلى الذخيرة لأنه إذا نوى أن يشرب ذلك فهو فاسق لم يتب بخلاف ما إذا قطع عنه فإنه فاسق تاب ومثله مقبول الشهادة وبه ينحل الإشكال تأمل .اهـ لكن في هوامش ابن الكمال المعزوة إليه بعد نقله ما في الذخيرة ولا يذهب عليك أنه أمر خفي لا يصلح أن يكون مداراً لعدم قبول الشهادة اهـ. ومثله في فتح القدير قوله : (وهو عجيب من محمد الخ) فيه نظر ظاهر يعلم مما أن الإدمان على شرب الخمر شرط لسقوط العدالة عند محمد مع قدمه عن الصدر الشهيد من يقول بأن مجرد شرب الخمر حرام ولو بدون إدمان وإسكار ولهذا قال المقدسي: وإنما فعل ذلك محمد يعني حيث اشترط الاعتياد على السكر من النبيذ للاحتياط فمنع القليل يعني من المسكر ولم يسقط
Book of Testimonies, Chapter: Whose Testimony is Accepted and Whose Not. And the habitual drinker for amusement, and whoever plays the tanbur or sings for people, or commits what necessitates [rejection]. A man litigated with a man over a house or a right, then this man testified against him in another matter – his testimony is permitted if he is upright. End. And know that if he testified against another man and that man litigated with him over something before the judgment, his testimony is still accepted unless he claims that he paid him such-and-such to avoid testifying against him, and he seeks rejection and proves his claim by evidence, confession, or refusal, then his testimony becomes void and it is an accepted accusation of bias, as they state, and it will come in the explanation of accusation. Fifth: If we say that the testimony of an enemy against his enemy is not permitted when it is worldly enmity, is the ruling regarding the judge the same, such that the judgment of the judge against someone with whom he has worldly enmity is not valid? I have not come across it in the books of our companions, and the answer should be detailed: If his judgment is based on his own knowledge, it should not be executed, but if it is based on the testimony of upright persons and in the presence of people in the court at the request of a legitimate opponent, it should be executed. Al-Mawardi from the Shafi'is distinguished between them by saying that the grounds for judgment are apparent while the grounds for testimony are hidden. End. His saying: (and the habitual drinker for amusement) meaning the testimony of one who persists in drinking what is not permissible to drink is not accepted. He used 'amusement' for the drink. Its apparent meaning is that addiction is necessary in the case of wine as well. In al-Khaniyyah: Addiction is required only so that it becomes apparent to people, for if someone is accused of drinking wine in his home, his uprightness is not invalidated even though it is a major sin; it is only invalidated if it becomes apparent or if he comes out drunk and children mock him, because such a person does not refrain from lying. The author in al-Kafi chose this, and in al-Nihayah, attributing to al-Dhakhirah: The testimony of a habitual wine drinker is not permitted. Then he said: with the condition of addiction, but he did not mean addiction in drinking, rather he meant addiction in intention, meaning he drinks with the intention to drink again if he finds it. And the testimony of one addicted to intoxication is not permitted, meaning intoxication from all drinks other than wine, because what is prohibited in other drinks is intoxication, so addiction to intoxication is required. Al-Ramli and Yaqub Pasha explicitly state in his marginalia the non-execution of the judge's judgment based on the testimony of an enemy against his enemy. And I say: Analogy requires the same for partisanship, so the judge's judgment based on his testimony should not be executed because he hates the man for being from so-and-so or from such a tribe, as will come soon in the margin from Mu'in al-Hukm, so ponder. His saying: (He used 'amusement' for the drink) etc. He said in al-Manh: This is contrary to the apparent meaning of the expression, because the apparent meaning is that 'habitual drinker for amusement' means one who persists in drinking wine for amusement. Al-Zayla'i said: meaning one who persists in drinking wine for the sake of amusement because drinking it is a major sin. Mulla Khusraw said: And the habitual drinker means drinking prohibited beverages, because addiction to other drinks does not invalidate testimony unless it is for amusement. End. So his words indicate that drinking for amusement is only a condition for non-prohibited drinks, but for prohibited ones it is not required, and this agrees with the statement of the author of al-Bahr. And it appears that this is what compelled him to interpret 'amusement' in Kanz as the drink, contrary to al-Zayla'i, who made it a condition for wine as well. The apparent view is the opposite, because drinking wine is a major sin that causes rejection of testimony whether drunk for amusement or not, and their apparent words indicate that addiction is necessary for wine. Seven: Addiction to intoxication, and what is prohibited in wine is the drinking itself, so addiction to drinking is required. End. The verification is contrary to both statements: that addiction by action or intention is not a condition for wine, because drinking even a large drop of it invalidates uprightness without insistence, and the scholars only mentioned addiction to make his drinking apparent to the judge, not as a condition, like their statement that a wailing woman's uprightness is not invalidated unless she wails at someone else's calamity, even though wailing is a major sin due to the threat, but it only becomes apparent at someone else's calamity usually. As for other than wine, addiction is necessary because drinking it is a minor sin, and the two statements about the interpretation of addiction are narrated in the interpretation of insistence upon it. Ibn al-Kamal mentioned that drinking wine is not a major sin, so uprightness is not invalidated except by insistence on it. He said in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: The uprightness of a wine drinker is not invalidated by the mere act of drinking because this ruling [of being a major sin] was not established by a definitive text unless he persists in it. End. This is contrary to the explicit statements of the scholars that its drinking is a major sin, and it contradicts the famous hadith on major sins which includes wine drinking. There is no implication in al-Sughra that it is a minor sin, as is clear, but there is a problem in its reasoning because the discussion is about the sin, not the hadd punishment; its prohibition is established by a conclusive proof, so they said: its legalizer becomes an infidel. And invalidation of uprightness is due to the drinking itself, not due to the hadd punishment. Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in Sharh Adab al-Qada: Al-Khassaf invalidated uprightness by wine drinking without addiction, while Muhammad required addiction for its invalidation, and it is the correct view. End. In al-Utabiyyah: The uprightness of people of good character is not invalidated by drinking unless it becomes well-known. In al-Zahiriyyah: Whoever becomes intoxicated from nabidh, his uprightness is invalidated according to al-Khassaf's statement because intoxication is prohibited by all. Muhammad said: His uprightness is not invalidated unless he becomes accustomed to it. End. This is strange from Muhammad because he declared a small amount of it prohibited yet did not invalidate uprightness with a large amount. Apparently, he holds that intoxication from it is a minor sin, so he required habituation. If you ask: Can a wine drinker testify if it is not known? I say: Yes, because in al-Multaqat: If someone is outwardly upright but secretly sinful, and the judge wants to rule based on his testimony, it is not permissible for him to mention his sin because that would expose his secret and invalidate the right of the claimant. End. There is no difference in intoxication that invalidates it also. End. His saying: (The verification is contrary to both statements.) Al-Ramli said in Hashiyat al-Manh: The goodness of what is in al-Nihayah attributed to al-Dhakhirah is not hidden, because if one intends to drink it, he is a sinner who has not repented, unlike if he stops. He is a sinner who has repented, and such a person's testimony is accepted, and by this the problem is resolved. Ponder. End. But in the marginalia of Ibn al-Kamal attributed to him after quoting from al-Dhakhirah: It should not escape you that this is a subtle matter that cannot be made the basis for non-acceptance of testimony. End. Similar in Fath al-Qadir. His saying: (And it is strange from Muhammad) etc. There is apparent reflection on it, known from the fact that addiction to wine drinking is a condition for invalidation of uprightness according to Muhammad, despite the earlier statement from al-Sadr al-Shahid that mere wine drinking is prohibited even without addiction or intoxication. Hence al-Maqdisi said: Muhammad only did this, i.e., required habituation to intoxication from nabidh, as a precaution, so he prohibited the small amount (of intoxicant) but did not invalidate uprightness with it.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, chapter on accepted testimony. It addresses two legal issues: (1) testimony of an enemy, and (2) testimony of a habitual drinker. For enemy testimony, the text discusses when enmity does not bar testimony: if the witness litigates against someone but later testifies in a different matter, his testimony is accepted unless the opponent proves that the witness paid him off to avoid earlier testimony. There is also a discussion on whether a judge can judge against his personal enemy. The author notes that he did not find a clear ruling in Hanafi sources, but suggests a distinction: if the judge rules based on his own knowledge, his judgment is not executed; if he rules based on public testimony in court, it is executed. A Shafi'i scholar distinguished between judgment (apparent reasons) and testimony (hidden reasons). (2) The main discussion is about the habitual drinker (مدمن الشرب على اللهو). The commentary clarifies the matn phrase: it refers to persistence in drinking prohibited beverages. For wine, the text investigates whether addiction (إدمان) is a condition for rejecting testimony. Some scholars require addiction to make it apparent to the judge; others require addiction in intention (continuing to drink when found). The author concludes that for wine, addiction is not a substantive condition because wine drinking is a major sin that invalidates uprightness even once. Addiction is only relevant for the judge's knowledge of the sin. For other intoxicants (like nabidh), addiction is necessary because drinking them without intoxication is a minor sin. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Khassaf held that even a single instance of wine drinking invalidates uprightness; Imam Muhammad required addiction. The relied-upon view (المعتمد) is Muhammad's, but many scholars side with al-Khassaf. The text also addresses the secret sinner: a witness who is outwardly upright but secretly sinful can still have his testimony accepted by the judge if his sin is not known, to avoid exposing secrets and harming the claimant's right.

Key Terms
مدمن الشرب (mudmin al-shurb): One who habitually drinks prohibited beverages; the text specifies addiction to drinking for amusement (على اللهو).
إدمان (idman): Habitual persistence in an act; in this context, either continuous drinking or intention to continue drinking when possible.
عدالة (adalah): Moral uprightness required for a witness's testimony to be accepted; nullified by committing major sins or persisting in minor sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of wine drinking on a witness's 'adala?
A) It only invalidates 'adala if the person is addicted.
B) It invalidates 'adala immediately because it is a major sin.
C) It invalidates 'adala only if the person drinks for amusement.
D) It never invalidates 'adala if the person repents.

Key Takeaway
Wine drinking is a major sin that immediately destroys a witness's 'adala, making addiction irrelevant; for other intoxicants, addiction is the key condition.

Shahadat — Testimony

Wine Drinking and Witness Disqualification: The Condition of Addiction
Explains the condition of addiction for disqualifying a witness due to wine drinking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on wine drinking and 'adalah, identify the mufta bihi, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, addiction to wine is required to disqualify a witness, not a single drink — a ruling based on istihsan and practicality.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of wine drinking on a witness's moral probity ( 'adalah). While all agree that wine is a major sin, the condition of addiction (idman) is debated. We will explore the positions of al-Khassaf and Imam Muhammad, and understand why the latter is relied upon. The key is distinguishing between the theoretical gravity of the sin and the practical need for clear evidence before disqualifying a witness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
What is the exact legal question? Does drinking wine alone disqualify a witness, or is addiction (idman) required? Pinpoint the point of contention.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between schools.
2
State the two positions
Summarize: Al-Khassaf holds that any drinking of wine disqualifies; Imam Muhammad requires addiction. Ibn al-Kamal even questioned whether wine is a major sin, but that is a minority view.
Imam Muhammad's position is from zahir al-riwayah.
3
Trace the proof for each
For al-Khassaf: wine is explicitly listed in the hadith of major sins, so one commission suffices. For Imam Muhammad: the hadith is accepted, but the condition of idman is derived from istihsan and the need for the judge to have clear, repetitive evidence.
The dalil is shared; the difference is in the application of istihsan.
4
Analyze the tarjih mechanism
Al-Sadr al-Shahid declares Imam Muhammad's view as sahih. The mufta bihi follows this because it avoids excessive rejection of witnesses and aligns with practical justice.
Tarjih based on ease (taysir) and reliability of evidence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a witness who drank only once is accepted under the mufta bihi, but would be rejected under al-Khassaf. This shows how the condition of idman affects real court cases.
This is the most important takeaway for application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of wine drinking on a witness's 'adalah (moral probity). The key issue is whether drinking wine alone disqualifies a witness, or whether addiction (idman) is required. The text presents a dispute within the Hanafi school. Al-Khassaf holds that any drinking of wine disqualifies, while Imam Muhammad requires addiction. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Imam Muhammad, as stated by al-Sadr al-Shahid. The proof includes a hadith listing major sins, including wine. The text also addresses the case of drinking for medicinal purposes and the distinction between wine and other intoxicants. The commentary by al-Ramli and others further clarifies the condition of sincerity and regret. The passage concludes with a note that sitting in gatherings of vice also affects 'adalah even without drinking.

Key Terms
الإدمان (al-idman): Addiction or habitual drinking; in this context, repeated consumption of wine such that it becomes a pattern.
عدالة (’adalah): Moral probity; the legal qualification required for a witness's testimony to be accepted.
شرب الخمر (shurb al-khamr): Drinking wine; considered a major sin in the Hanafi school based on hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (al-mufta bihi), when does a wine drinker's testimony become invalid?
A) Immediately upon the first drink
B) Only if he drinks to intoxication
C) Only if he is addicted (idman) to drinking
D) His testimony is always invalid

Key Takeaway
Addiction (idman) is the decisive factor for disqualifying a wine-drinking witness in the Hanafi school, reflecting a balance between legal purity and practical necessity.

Playing the Tunbur and Its Effect on Witness Testimony
Ruling on witness testimony and frivolous activities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of testimony from a person who plays the tunbur and identify the criteria that disqualify a witness.

Core Idea
A witness becomes disqualified if he engages in activities considered lowly (sheni') by sound custom.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on whether playing musical instruments, specifically the tunbur, invalidates a person's testimony. The key determinant is whether the activity is considered repugnant (sheni') by people of upright custom (urf). The hadith 'ma min dadd' provides the textual proof against frivolous play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins with 'And whoever plays the tunbur' – meaning his testimony is not accepted. Determine the hukm for playing the tunbur: it is a cause for rejection of testimony.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith 'ma min dadd wa la al-dadd minni' to show that frivolous play is not from the Prophet's way, implying it diminishes moral character ('adalah).
The hadith is used as a general proof against heedless amusement.
3
Distinguish between sheni' and non-sheni' activities
Not all play disqualifies. The text distinguishes between socially lowly acts (e.g., playing tunbur) and harmless ones (e.g., keeping pigeons for company). Apply the urf (custom) to classify the activity.
Sheni' means repugnant in the eyes of reputable people.
4
Apply to related cases
The passage expands to other activities: beating the rod (non-sheni') is permissible unless it becomes excessive with dancing; playing with polo sticks for horsemanship is allowed. Consider the degree of heedlessness and social disapproval.

Elevated places and selling? He said: (And whoever plays the tunbur) meaning his testimony is not accepted, and it is interpreted in al-Hidayah as the singer, and in another copy as birds because it causes heedlessness, and it is interpreted as when he stands on the roof to release his pigeon to see women's private parts. As for keeping pigeons in his house for companionship, it does not disqualify because keeping them in houses is permissible. (continues with full translation)

Commentary
This passage rules on the admissibility of testimony from those who engage in frivolous activities, specifically playing the tunbur (a string instrument). The key distinction is whether the activity is considered lowly (sheni') in the eyes of reputable people. Playing instruments that are associated with heedlessness and immorality, such as the tunbur, disqualifies a witness. However, keeping pigeons for pleasure or carrying letters is permissible unless it leads to trespassing. The hadith 'ma min dadd wa la al-dadd minni' is cited to discourage frivolous play. The ruling also covers playing with other instruments: if it becomes excessive and involves dancing, it becomes a major sin. The ultimate criterion is whether the activity distracts from religious obligations and whether it is socially disapproved.

Key Terms
الطنبور (al-tunbur): A stringed musical instrument; in this context, any instrument that is considered lowly and associated with frivolity.
شنيع (sheni'): Repugnant or lowly in the eyes of people; something socially disapproved.
الدد (al-dadd): Play and amusement; used in the hadith to condemn frivolous activity.

Singing disqualifies a witness because it is a major sin, whether done publicly or privately.

Why Addiction is Required for Wine Drinking to Disqualify a Witness
Question-answer explaining rationale for a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires addiction (idman) for wine drinking to disqualify a witness, and evaluate the role of istihsan and haraj in this ruling.

Core Idea
Wine drinking requires addiction to disqualify because it is common; requiring idman avoids widespread hardship.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq answers a critical question: why does wine drinking need addiction when other hadd offenses do not? The answer reveals a key Hanafi principle—istihsan based on haraj. Understanding this rationale deepens your appreciation of how the Hanafi school balances textual rulings with practical considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in question
The ruling is that a witness must be an addict (idman) for wine drinking to disqualify his 'adalah (uprightness). For other hadd offenses, a single commission suffices.
2
Recognize the potential objection
One might ask: why treat wine differently from theft or fornication? The passage sets up this objection to highlight the need for a rationale.
This objection likely comes from a hypothetical interlocutor.
3
Understand the rationale: frequency of occurrence
Al-Birjandi explains that wine drinking occurs more frequently than other hadd offenses. If mere drinking disqualified, many people would lose their testimony, causing widespread hardship.
Frequency is the empirical basis for the distinction.
4
Apply the principle of haraj (hardship)
The concept of haraj is a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul: rulings that lead to excessive hardship are mitigated. Here, requiring idman prevents the hardship of mass disqualification.
This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.
5
Connect to the broader Hanafi method
This passage exemplifies how Hanafi jurists sometimes restrict a general rule based on practical consequences. The condition of idman is not arbitrary but based on a valid rationale.
Compare with other rulings where frequency affects the law (e.g., istihsan in transactions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت لم اشترط الإدمان في الشرب دون غيره مما يوجب الحد؟ قلت : ذكر البرجندي أن الوقوع في الشرب أكثر من الوقوع في غيره فلو جعل مجرد الشرب مسقطاً للعدالة أدى إلى الحرج اهـ. أبو السعود.
If you say: Why was addiction required in drinking and not in other things that necessitate the hadd punishment? I say: Al-Birjandi mentioned that occurrence in drinking is more than occurrence in other things, so if mere drinking were made a disqualifier of 'adalah, it would lead to hardship. End quote. Abu al-Sa'ud.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This is a question-answer that explains the wisdom behind the Hanafi position requiring addiction (idman) for wine drinking to disqualify a witness, while other hadd offenses (like theft, fornication) do not require addiction. The reason given by al-Birjandi is that wine drinking is more widespread; if every single instance of drinking disqualified a witness, it would cause great hardship (haraj). Therefore, the condition of addiction limits the disqualification to habitual drinkers, making the ruling practical.

Key Terms
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship or difficulty; a principle for leniency in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because wine drinking is never a major sin
B) Because it is difficult to prove
C) Because wine drinking is more common, and requiring idman avoids hardship
D) Because Muhammad simply stated it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi requirement of idman for wine drinking is based on istihsan to avoid hardship, not on textual distinction.

Ruling on Testimony of One Who Drinks or Sits with Sinners
Clarifying the effect of drinking and sinful company on a witness's probity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a witness who drinks alcohol or sits with sinners, and explain why probity is affected.

Core Idea
A witness's probity is compromised by association with sin, even without active participation.

This lesson examines a passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that addresses the effect of drinking and sinful company on a witness's moral probity ('adalah). The Hanafi school holds that both actions can disqualify a witness, though there is a nuanced exception for medicinal use. Understanding this ruling is crucial for evaluating the integrity of testimony in Islamic courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key legal concept
The central concept is 'adalah (probity), which is a condition for valid testimony. The passage explains what actions remove probity.
'Adalah is defined as 'a state of religious and moral uprightness that prevents one from committing major sins or persisting in minor sins.
2
State the ruling for a Muslim witness who drinks
The passage clearly indicates that drinking alcohol (khamr) disqualifies a witness because it is a major sin that destroys probity. Even a dhimmi's testimony is rejected if drunk.
The source references al-Multaqat: 'When a dhimmi gets drunk, his testimony is not accepted.'
3
State the ruling for a Muslim witness who sits with drinkers without drinking
The commentator explains that even sitting in assemblies of vice and drinking (majalis al-fujur wa al-shurb) disqualifies a witness, because he resembles sinners and becomes careless about avoiding false testimony.
The Arabic phrase 'تشبه بهم' (tashabbaha bihim) indicates that resemblance to sinners is enough to erode caution.
4
Identify the exception for medicinal use
The passage notes an exception: if a person drinks alcohol for treatment (li al-tadawi), it does not remove probity because there is room for ijtihad regarding its permissibility when necessary.
This exception is attributed to Ibn al-Kamal, a late Hanafi authority.
5
Compare the cross-madhhab view
The Hanafi position that mere association disqualifies is contested. For example, the Shafi'i school holds that probity is only lost by committing sins, not by association alone.
Understanding this ikhtilaf is important for evaluating testimony in different legal contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين المسلم والذمي لما في الملتقط : وإذا سكر الذمي لا تقبل شهادته. وفي المصباح: اللهو معروف وأصله ترويح النفس بما لا تقتضيه الحكمة ا هـ . وذكر الشارح لا تقبل شهادة من يجلس مجالس الفجور والشرب وإن لم يشرب لأنه تشبه بهم ولا يحترز أن يظهر عليه ما يظهر عليهم فلا يحترز عن شهادة الزور ا هـ . وفي قوله «على اللهو» إشارة إلى أنه لو شربها للتداوي لم تسقط عدالته لأن للاجتهاد فيه مساغاً ذكره ابن الكمال.
(Passage discussing) between the Muslim and the dhimmi: As stated in al-Multaqat: When a dhimmi gets drunk, his testimony is not accepted. And in al-Misbah: Lahw (amusement) is known, its origin is refreshing the soul with that which wisdom does not require. The commentator said: The testimony of one who sits in assemblies of vice and drinking is not accepted even if he does not drink, because he resembles them and does not guard against what appears upon them, so he does not guard against false testimony. And in his saying 'upon amusement' is an indication that if he drinks it for treatment, his probity does not fall because there is room for ijtihad in it; mentioned by Ibn al-Kamal.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the effect of drinking and associating with sinners on a witness's 'adalah (moral probity). The author, Ibn al-Kamal, notes that if a dhimmi becomes intoxicated, his testimony is rejected. More importantly, even sitting in places of vice and drinking (majalis al-fujur wa al-shurb) without actually drinking disqualifies a witness, because he becomes akin to the sinners and loses his cautiousness against false testimony. However, there is an exception: if one drinks alcohol for medicinal purposes (li al-tadawi), it does not remove probity because there is room for ijtihad regarding its permissibility when necessary. This ruling highlights that the primary concern is the moral integrity and caution of the witness.

Key Terms
عدالة ('adalah): Moral probity required for a witness's testimony to be accepted

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because he resembles sinners and may become careless about false testimony
C) Because it is forbidden to sit in such places
D) Because he loses his memory

Key Takeaway
Association with sin, even without participation, can compromise a witness's probity and disqualify testimony in the Hanafi school.

Ruling on Testimony of One Who Plays the Tunbur and Similar Amusements
Determining when playing musical instruments affects a witness's probity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a person who plays musical instruments, and apply the conditions that determine whether such amusement invalidates probity.

Core Idea
Playing disgraceful amusements invalidates testimony; harmless leisure does not.

Can a person who plays a musical instrument or keeps pigeons be a witness? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: the key is whether the amusement is disgraceful (shani‘) and whether it distracts from religious duties. This lesson unpacks the conditions from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, showing how seemingly similar activities can lead to different outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Start with the author's statement: testifying against a person who plays the tunbur (or similar amusements) is rejected. Understand that this is the default position.
2
Examine the exceptions
Note the exceptions: keeping pigeons for companionship (isti'nas) or for carrying letters does not affect probity. However, if the pigeons attract others' pigeons and the owner eats them, probity falls.
The exception hinges on the permissibility of the activity and absence of harm.
3
Understand the qualifier 'disgraceful' (shani‘)
The author interprets 'tunbur' broadly as any disgraceful amusement. Contrast with non-disgraceful acts like striking a staff (darb al-qadib) or singing for camels (hida')—these are permitted unless they become excessive (e.g., dancing).
The degree of social disapproval matters.
4
Apply the distraction test
If the amusement distracts from obligatory duties (fara’id), it invalidates probity. If it does not distract but is still disgraceful, the testimony is still rejected. If it is neither disgraceful nor distracting, probity remains.
This test comes from al-Khaniyyah.
5
Consider the hadith evidence
The mashayikh cite a marfu‘ hadith: 'There is no dad, nor is dad from me.' 'Dad' means amusement/laughter. The Prophet’s disassociation strengthens the prohibition against idle play.
The hadith is mursal (zanni), but supported by the school's reliance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن يلعب بالطنبور) أي لا تقبل شهادته وفسره في الهداية بالمغني وفي نسخة أخرى بالطيور لأنه يورث غفلة وهو محمول على ما إذا كان يقف على عورات النساء لصعوده سطحه ليطير طيره، فأما إمساك الحمام في بيته للاستئناس لا يسقطها لأن إمساكها في البيوت مباح؛ كذا في النهاية وزاد في المعراج أن إمساكها لحمل الكتب كما في ديار مصر والشام مباح إلا إن كانت تجر حمامات أخر مملوكة لغيره فتفرخ في وكرها فيأكل لأنه ملك الغير ولا يحل له فسقطت عدالته؛ كذا ذكر الشارح يعني وإن لم يقف على السطح كما في المعراج وأراد المؤلف بالطنبور كل لهو كان شنيعاً بين الناس احترازاً عما لم يكن شنيعاً كضرب القضيب فإنه لا يمنع قبولها إلا أن يتفاحش بأن يرقصوا به فيدخل في حد الكبائر؛ كذا في المحيط. وقد ذكر المشايخ هنا حديثاً مرفوعاً «ما من دد ولا الدد مني قال في الصحاح : الدد اللهو واللعب وفيه ثلاث لغات تقول هذا دد وددا مثل نقا وددن ا هـ . وذكر القطب في حاشية الكشاف من سورة النساء الدد اللهو واللعب والتنكير في دد للشيوع أي ما أنا في شيء من اللهو والتعريف في الدد للعهد كأنه قال ولا ذلك النوع مني ا هـ . وذكر الكرماني من شركات شرح البخاري أن «من» في الحديث تسمى اتصالية. وفي الولوالجية اللاعب بالصولجان يريد به الفروسية جازت شهادته لأنه غير محظور ا.هـ. وفي الخانية : وإن لعب بشيء من الملاهي ولم يشغله ذلك عن الفرائض لا تبطل عدالته، والملاعبة بالأهل والفرس لا تبطل العدالة ما لم يمنعه ذلك عن الفرائض، فإن كان اللعب بالملاهي لا يشغله عنها إلا أنه شنيع بين الناس كالمزامير والطنابير فكذلك، وإن لم يكن شنيعاً نحو الحداء وضرب القضيب لا إلا إذا فحش بأن كانوا يرقصون عند ذلك اهـ.
His saying (and whoever plays the tunbur): i.e., his testimony is not accepted. In al-Hidayah it is interpreted as al-mughanni (the singer), and in another copy as al-tuyur (birds), because it causes heedlessness. This is understood as when he stands over the private parts of women by climbing his roof to release his bird. As for keeping pigeons in his house for companionship, it does not remove probity because keeping them in houses is permissible; thus in al-Nihayah. And in al-Mi'raj it adds that keeping them to carry letters, as in the lands of Egypt and Syria, is permissible, unless they attract other pigeons belonging to someone else and they lay eggs in its nest, and he eats them—because that is the property of another and not permissible for him, so his probity falls. The commentator said this even if he does not stand on the roof, as in al-Mi'raj. The author intended by tunbur every amusement that is disgraceful (shani') among people, in contrast to what is not disgraceful, like striking a staff (darb al-qadib), for that does not prevent acceptance unless it becomes excessive such that they dance with it, entering the category of major sins; thus in al-Muhit. The mashayikh mentioned here a marfu' hadith: 'There is no dad, nor is dad from me.' In al-Sihah: dad means amusement and play; it has three languages: one says hadha dad and dadan like naqa and dadan. And al-Qutb mentioned in his hashiah on al-Kashshaf from Surah al-Nisa': dad means amusement and play, and the tanwin in dad indicates generality, meaning I am not in any amusement, and the definite article in al-dad indicates a specific type, as if he said, 'and not that type from me.' And al-Kirmani mentioned in his commentary on Bukhari that 'min' in the hadith is called 'ittisaliyyah (connective). In al-Walwaljiyyah: one who plays with the sawlajan (polo stick) intending horsemanship, his testimony is permitted because it is not prohibited. In al-Khaniyyah: if one plays with something of amusement and it does not distract him from obligatory duties, his probity is not invalidated. Playing with one's family and horse does not invalidate probity as long as it does not prevent him from obligatory duties. If the play with amusements does not distract him but it is disgraceful among people, like flutes and tunburs, then the same (it invalidates). And if it is not disgraceful, like singing for camels (hida') and striking the staff, then no (it does not invalidate) unless it becomes excessive such that they dance with it.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the ruling on a witness who plays the tunbur (a type of lute) or similar amusements. The commentator explains that playing the tunbur is generally a cause for rejecting testimony because it leads to heedlessness (ghafla). However, the ruling is nuanced: if one keeps pigeons or birds for lawful purposes like companionship or carrying messages, it does not remove probity. But if the pigeons attract others' pigeons and the owner eats them (unlawfully), probity falls. The author interprets 'tunbur' broadly to include any disgraceful amusement (lahw shani'), while excluding harmless acts like striking a staff (darb al-qadib) unless accompanied by dancing, which becomes a major sin. The passage also cites a hadith about 'dad' (amusement) to emphasize the Prophetic aversion to idle play. Depending on the context, playing polo (sawlajan) for horsemanship is allowed. Ultimately, the key factor is whether the amusement is disgraceful or distracts from obligatory duties.

Key Terms
طنبور (tunbur): A stringed musical instrument; metaphor for any disgraceful amusement
عدالة ('adalah): Moral probity required for testimony; lost by committing major sins or persisting in minor ones

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does playing with birds not affect a witness's probity?
A) If the birds are trained for hunting
B) If they are kept for companionship (isti'nas) in the house
C) If the witness climbs to the roof to fly them
D) If the witness plays with them in public

Key Takeaway
Playing the tunbur or similar disgraceful amusements invalidates testimony, but harmless leisure activities like keeping pigeons for companionship do not.

When Does Singing Disqualify a Witness? (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on singing and probity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi ruling on when singing affects a witness's probity and distinguish the conditions based on intention and instrument.

Core Idea
Singing for oneself to remove loneliness does not disqualify; singing with instruments or for amusement does.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates a classic intra-Hanafi disagreement: does singing automatically destroy probity (adalah) and thus invalidate testimony? The answer hinges on intention, audience, and instruments. By parsing the opinions of Shaykh al-Islam, al-Sarakhsi, and al-Kafi, the author settles on a nuanced view: singing for oneself is fine; singing for people or with instruments is prohibited and removes probity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its effect
The act under discussion is singing (ghina'). The question is whether it removes probity (adalah), making testimony invalid. Begin by noting that probity is defined as abstaining from major sins (kaba'ir).
Probity is a condition for witness acceptance in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Locate the main opinions
Extract the three positions: (1) Shaykh al-Islam: any singing is a major sin, even alone. (2) Al-Sarakhsi: only singing for amusement (lahw) is prohibited. (3) Al-Kafi: singing to oneself to remove loneliness is permitted.
These are all Hanafi authorities, showing an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the evidence
The prohibition is based on a hadith narrating the Prophet's command to eradicate musical instruments (ma'azif). The author also cites al-Ziyadat and consensus on instrument-assisted singing. The key dalil is the hadith of Abu Umamah.
The hadith is ahad (zanni thubut), so it allows for interpretation.
4
Recognize the tarjih (mufta bihi)
Ibn al-Kamal and al-Ayni support that singing for oneself to dispel loneliness does not remove probity. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq adopts this as the relied-upon view, reconciling earlier texts.
This is confirmed by al-Binayah and al-Inayah: singing for amusement is sinful, not absolute singing.
5
Apply to scenarios
Now distinguish: a witness who occasionally hums to himself while working retains probity. One who sings with a lute at a party loses probity. One who sings at a wedding with duff (tambourine) is within permissible limits but should be careful of excess.
The modern equivalent: karaoke with instruments is problematic; a cappella for personal mood is fine.

(من سرور كبيرة، ويمكن حمله على ما قاله السرخسي بأن يكون كبيرة بسبب الاجتماع عليه ويؤيده كلام المصنف في الكافي وهو المتبادر من لفظ يغني للناس وعلى ذلك حمله في العناية. ويؤيده ما يأتي في الهامش عن ابن الكمال والعيني من أ أنه لو كان لنفسه ليزيل الوحشة عنها لا تسقط عدالته في الصحيح فهذا التصحيح موافق لهذا المتن كغيره من المتون فكان عليه المعوّل فلا تغفل . قوله: (فقد ثبت لص المذهب على حرمته إن أراد أنه حرام مطلقاً فهو مخالف لما حمله عليه في البناية والعناية فإنهما استدلا بعبارة لزيادات على أنه معصية لقصد اللهو فلم يجرياه على عمومه فهو
His saying: (or he sings for the people) because it gathers people upon committing a major sin; thus in al-Hidayah. Its apparent meaning is that singing is a major sin even if not for people, rather for making himself hear to remove loneliness; this is the saying of Shaykh al-Islam. Probity (falls) except if he becomes accustomed and does not suffice with frequency? (text unclear). Ibn al-Kamal: if it is not for people, only for himself to remove loneliness, his testimony is not rejected; and it is said that he is not relied upon. Al-Walwalji: it is not permissible except if he does not make it a habit and does not suffice with frequency? (unclear). If you say: why is addiction (idman) required in drinking but not in other things that necessitate hadd? I say: al-Birjandi mentioned that falling into drinking is more frequent than falling into other things, so if mere drinking were made to remove probity, it would lead to hardship (haraj). Abu al-Sa'ud. His saying: (and its apparent meaning is that singing is a major sin even if not for people) because he made the singing that gathers people upon it as the general prohibition. And Imam al-Sarakhsi only prohibited what is by way of amusement. Some permitted it for people in a wedding or walima. Some permitted it for making oneself hear to remove loneliness. Some permitted it to benefit from the composition of poetry and eloquence of tongue. It is surprising that the author in al-Kafi gave the same reason as in al-Hidayah but permitted it if it is for making oneself hear to remove loneliness. In Fath al-Qadir: prohibited singing (taghanni) is that whose words are unlawful, like describing a specific living man or woman, describing wine and inciting to it, descriptions of monasteries and taverns, and satire of a Muslim or dhimmi if the speaker intends satire; not if he intends to compose poetry for citation or to learn eloquence and rhetoric. He said further: In al-Ajnas, Muhammad ibn Shuja' was asked about one who hums (yatarannam) to himself; he said it does not harm his testimony. As for recitation (al-Qur'an) with tunes (al-alhan), some permitted it, some forbade it. The chosen view is that if the tunes do not take the letters out of their proper order and norms, it is permissible; otherwise not. The author of Fath al-Qadir noted that we already mentioned in the chapter on adhan that chanting (talhin) only occurs with alteration of the letters' requirements, so this distinction is meaningless. In al-Mi'raj: amusements are of two types: forbidden (muharram) are musical instruments that are not singing, like the flute (mizmar) whether of wood or reed like the shababa, or others like the lute ('ud) and tunbur, because Abu Umamah narrated that the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said: 'Indeed Allah sent me as a mercy to the worlds and commanded me to eradicate musical instruments and flutes' and because it is absorbing and prevents the remembrance of Allah. The second type is permissible: the duff (tambourine) in weddings, and whatever is analogous to it, and it is disliked in other contexts, because it is reported from Umar (رضي الله عنه) that when he heard the sound of the duff, he would send and look: if it was for a wedding, he kept quiet; if for other, he struck with the whip. And it is disliked for men in all cases due to resemblance to women. This is quoted in Fath al-Qadir without critique. Al-Bazzazi in al-Manaqib reported consensus on the prohibition of singing when accompanied by an instrument like the lute; as for without instrument, the difference is known. The commentators did not specify the madhhab. In al-Binayah and al-Inayah: singing for amusement (taghanni li al-lahw) is a sin in all religions. In al-Ziyadat: if one bequeaths something that is a sin according to us and the People of the Book, and among them is bequest to male and female singers, especially if from a woman. So the text of the madhhab is established on its prohibition, ending the difference. In Diya' al-Hulum: al-ghina' (singing) is a sound of the singer, while al-ghina (wealth) is abundance of wealth; the first is mamdud (elongated), the second is maqsur (short). (From surur kabira... unclear). It can be interpreted as what al-Sarakhsi said: that it is a major sin due to gathering upon it. And the speech of the author in al-Kafi supports it, and the apparent meaning of the phrase 'he sings for people' leans towards that, and upon that it is interpreted in al-Inayah. And it is supported by what comes in the margin from Ibn al-Kamal and al-Ayni: that if it is for himself to remove loneliness, his probity does not fall in the correct view. Thus this correction agrees with this matn as with other matn, so it is the relied-upon view; do not neglect. His saying: (so the text of the madhhab is established on its prohibition) if he intends that it is absolutely prohibited, it contradicts what is carried in al-Binayah and al-Inayah, for they both adduced the expression of al-Ziyadat that it is a sin with the intention of amusement, and they did not apply it generally.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on singing (ghina') and its effect on a witness's probity. The author clarifies that the apparent meaning of al-Hidayah is that singing is a major sin (kabira) even if done privately, because it gathers people upon sin. However, there is a spectrum of opinions: Imam al-Sarakhsi restricted prohibition to singing done for amusement (li al-lahw). Some scholars permitted singing at weddings or to oneself for dispelling loneliness. The commentator notes a contradiction in some sources: al-Kafi permitted self-singing while al-Hidayah seemed to prohibit absolutely. The passage also cites Fath al-Qadir, which distinguishes based on content: unlawful singing includes satire, descriptions of wine, etc., while poetry for eloquence is fine. A critical point is the classification of musical instruments in al-Mi'raj: forbidden instruments are those that are musical (like flutes, lutes, tunbur) because of the hadith commanding eradication of such instruments; permissible is the duff (tambourine) at weddings, otherwise disliked. The text then cites al-Bazzazi claiming consensus on prohibition when instruments are used, but difference remains on a cappella singing. However, the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq notes that the relied-upon view (mu'tamad) in the Hanafi madhhab is that singing for amusement (taghanni li al-lahw) is a sin, but if done privately to remove loneliness, probity is not affected. The passage ends by reconciling apparent contradictions: the 'text of the madhhab' on prohibition should be understood as conditional on amusement intention, not absolute.

Key Terms
غناء (ghina'): Singing; refers to vocal music, often with or without instruments
لهو (lahw): Amusement; any activity that distracts from religious duties
معازف (ma'azif): Musical instruments; specifically stringed or wind instruments that produce rhythm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, when does singing not affect a witness's probity?
A) If he sings for people at a wedding
B) If he sings to himself to remove loneliness without sinful content
C) If he sings with a lute ('ud)
D) If he sings satire against a Muslim

Key Takeaway
Singing without instruments and without sinful content, done privately to remove loneliness, does not remove a witness's probity in the mufta bihi Hanafi view.

Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
12 min
Ruling on Singing and Its Effect on Testimony
Summarizes multiple scholarly views on singing and its effect on witness reliability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on singing with and without instruments, and explain how it affects the testimony of a witness.

Core Idea
Singing with instruments is haram and disqualifies testimony; without instruments, the ruling depends on content and intent.

The issue of singing (ghina) and its impact on a witness's reliability is a nuanced topic in Hanafi fiqh. While many assume all singing is permissible, the Hanafi school makes a careful distinction between instrumental and non-instrumental singing, and between lawful and unlawful content. This lesson examines the key texts and scholarly opinions, culminating in the relied-upon position of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon: singing with instruments, without instruments, and the intent (amusement, worship, etc.).
The ruling varies based on these factors.
2
Locate the primary evidence
Find the hadith cited (from Abu Umamah) that commands abolishing musical instruments, and understand its implication.
Hadith: 'Allah sent me to abolish musical instruments and flutes.'
3
Examine the madhhab positions
Review the different Hanafi opinions: al-Sarakhsi limiting prohibition to amusement, others permitting at weddings or for personal relief, and the text of al-Ziyadat declaring it haram.
The dispute is resolved by the madhhab text.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify which position is relied upon: singing with instruments is haram; without instruments it is haram if for amusement or with unlawful content.
Based on al-Ziyadat and the consensus on instruments.
5
Apply to testimony
Understand how the ruling affects witness reliability: a person who engages in prohibited singing is rejected as a witness; if permissible, testimony is accepted.
Specific to the act of singing, not character generally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات باب من تقبل شهادته لا تقبل ومن هو في قال بعموم المنع، والإمام السرخسي إنما منع ما كان على سبيل اللهو. ومنهم من جوزه للناس في عرس أو وليمة، ومنهم من جوّزه لإسماع نفسه دفعاً للوحشة، ومنهم من جوزه ليستفيد به نظم القوافي وفصاحة اللسان والعجب من المصنف في الكافي أنه علل بما علل به في الهداية وجوّزه إذا كان لإسماع نفسه إزالة للوحشة وفي فتح القدير : التغني المحرم ما كان اللفظ ما لا يحل كصفة الذكر والمرأة المعينة الحية ووصف الخمر والمهيج إليها والديريات والحانات والهجاء لمسلم أو ذمي إذا أراد المتكلم هجاءه لا إذا أراد إنشاء الشعر للاستشهاد به أو لتعلم فصاحة وبلاغة إلى أن قال : وفي الأجناس سئل محمد بن شجاع عن الذي يترنم مع نفسه قال : لا يقدح في شهادته وأما القراءة بالألحان فأباحها قوم وحظرها قوم، والمختار إن كانت الألحان لا تخرج الحروف عن نظمها وقدوراتها فمباح وإلا فغير مباح ؛ كذا ذكر وقدمنا في باب الأذان ما يفيد أن التلحين لا يكون إلا مع تغيير مقتضيات الحروف فلا معنى لهذا التفصيل ا هـ . وفي المعراج الملاهي نوعان : محرم وهو الآلات المطربة من غير الغناء كالمزمار سواء كان من عود أو قصب كالشبابة أو غيره كالعود والطنبور لما روى أبو إمامة أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال إن الله بعثني رحمة للعالمين وأمرني بمحق المعازف والمزامير ولأنه مطرب مصد عن ذكر الله تعالى والنوع الثاني مباح وهو الدف في النكاح وفي معناه ما كان حادث ويكره في غيره لما روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه أنه لما سمع صوت الدف بعث فنظر فإن كان فيه وليمة سكت وإن كان في غيره عمده بالدرة، وهو مكروه للرجال على كل حال للتشبه بالنساء ا هـ . ونقله في فتح القدير ولم يتعقبه، ونقل البزازي في المناقب الإجماع على حرمة الغناء إذا كان على آلة كالعود وأما إذا كان بغيرها فقد علمت الاختلاف، ولم يصرح الشارحون بالمذهب. وفي البناية والعناية : التغني للهو معصية في جميع الأديان. قال في الزيادات : إذا أوصى بما هو معصية عندنا وعند أهل الكتاب وذكر منها الوصية للمغنيين والمغنيات خصوصاً إذا كان من المرأة هـ. فقد ثبت نص المذهب على حرمته فانقطع الاختلاف. وفي ضياء الحلوم الغناء على وزن فعال صوت المغني والغنى كثرة المال اهـ . فالأول ممدود والثاني مقصور.
Book of testimonies, chapter on whose testimony is accepted and whose is not. He said with general prohibition, and Imam al-Sarakhsi only prohibited what was for amusement. Some permitted it for people at a wedding or feast, some permitted it for hearing oneself to dispel loneliness, some permitted it to benefit from the organization of rhymes and eloquence of tongue. It is surprising from the author in al-Kafi that he reasoned with what was reasoned in al-Hidayah and permitted it if it is for hearing oneself to remove loneliness. In Fath al-Qadir: prohibited singing (taghanni) is when the wording is unlawful, like describing the male or a specific living woman, describing wine and inciting to it, monasteries and taverns, satire of a Muslim or dhimmi if the speaker intends satire, not if he intends composing poetry for citation or to learn eloquence and rhetoric. He said: in al-Ajnas: Muhammad ibn Shuja' was asked about one who hums to himself: he said: it does not impair his testimony. As for recitation with melodies, some permitted it and some prohibited it. The chosen opinion is if the melodies do not remove the letters from their order and measures then it is permissible, otherwise not permissible. This was mentioned, and we have previously stated in the chapter on adhan that melodic singing (talhin) only occurs with changing the requirements of the letters, so this detail is meaningless. In al-Mi'raj: musical instruments are of two types: prohibited: which are the instruments producing rhythm without singing, like the flute, whether from wood or reed like the pipe or others like the lute and tambour, because Abu Umamah reported that the Prophet said: 'Allah sent me as a mercy to the worlds and commanded me to abolish musical instruments and flutes'; and because it is rhythmic and distracts from the remembrance of Allah. The second type is permissible: the hand-drum (duff) at weddings, and similarly what may happen; and it is disliked in other than that, because it is reported that 'Umar when he heard the sound of a hand-drum he would send and look: if it was a wedding feast he remained silent; if it was other than that he would beat it with a whip. It is disliked for men in any case due to resembling women. Al-Bazzazi in al-Manaqiq narrated consensus on the prohibition of singing if with an instrument like the lute; as for without an instrument, the difference is known. The commentators did not make the madhhab explicit. In al-Binayah and al-'Inayah: singing for amusement is a sin in all religions. Al-Ziyadat says: if one bequeaths something that is a sin according to us and according to the People of the Book, and among them he mentioned the bequest to male and female singers, especially if it is from a woman. Thus the text of the madhhab has been established on its prohibition, so the difference is cut off. In Diya' al-Huloom: al-ghina' (singing) on the measure of fi'al is the voice of the singer, and al-ghana (wealth) is abundance of wealth. So the first is with madd (elongated) and the second is with qasr (shortened).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of the ruling on singing (ghina) and its impact on the testimony of the witness. It opens by noting a general prohibition but then qualifies it: Imam al-Sarakhsi only prohibits singing intended for amusement. Several opinions follow: some permit singing at weddings and feasts; some permit it for personal listening to relieve loneliness; some permit it for learning poetic meters and eloquence. The author expresses surprise at al-Kafi's reasoning which follows al-Hidayah and permits it for personal relief. Then Fath al-Qadir is quoted: prohibited singing is that whose content is unlawful—describing men or specific women, praising wine, inciting to it, describing monasteries and taverns, or satirizing Muslims and dhimmis with intent. If the poetry is for citation or learning eloquence, it is not prohibited. Muhammad ibn Shuja' states that humming to oneself does not impair testimony. Regarding recitation with melodies: some allow, some forbid; the chosen view is that if melodies do not distort the letters and their sounds, it is permissible, otherwise not. However, the author criticizes this, noting that in the chapter on adhan it was established that such melodic alteration (talhin) always distorts. In al-Mi'raj, musical instruments are divided into two: prohibited (any rhythmic instrument like flute, lute, tambour) based on a hadith, and permissible (the hand-drum at weddings) based on 'Umar's practice. The hand-drum is disliked for men due to imitation of women. Al-Bazzazi reports consensus on prohibition when instruments are used; without instruments, there is disagreement. Al-Binayah and al-'Inayah state singing for amusement is a sin in all religions. Al-Ziyadat mentions that bequeathing to singers is invalid because it is a sin in Islamic law and the law of the People of the Book. Thus the madhhab text establishes prohibition, settling the dispute. The passage ends by distinguishing the words ghina' (singing, with elongated alif) and ghina (wealth, with shortened alif). The lesson shows that while the ruling on singing is nuanced, the relied-upon position of the Hanafi school is that singing with instruments is prohibited and impairs testimony, while singing without instruments is permissible if content is lawful and not for amusement.

Key Terms
الغناء (al-ghinā'): Singing; the voice of the singer, often with tune.
المعازف (al-ma‘āzif): Musical instruments, especially those that produce rhythm.
الدف (al-duff): Hand-drum, allowed at weddings.
التغني (al-taghannī): Singing with melody; sometimes used for Quranic recitation with melody.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following types of singing does the Hanafi madhhab consider to be haram and thus grounds for rejecting a witness's testimony?
A) Humming poetry to oneself to relieve loneliness
B) Reciting the Quran with a beautiful voice without distorting letters
C) Singing with a lute (oud) at a party
D) Singing without instruments at a wedding, with lawful words

Key Takeaway
Singing with instruments is haram and disqualifies a witness; singing without instruments is evaluated based on content and intent, and does not necessarily disqualify.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
