Definition of Major Sin (Kabira) and Its Effect on Testimony
Defines major sin and its impact on witness justice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a major sin (kabira) and apply it to determine whether a witness's testimony is admissible.

Core Idea
A major sin is defined by abominableness, sanctity violation, opposition to muru'ah, or persistence; its commission disqualifies a witness.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles a critical issue in testimony law: what exactly makes a sin 'major' enough to strip a witness of their legal integrity ('adalah). The Hanafi school offers a nuanced definition that goes beyond the mere presence of a hadd punishment, incorporating social and moral dimensions. Understanding this definition is essential for anyone dealing with court procedure or evaluating the reliability of witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject and its ruling
The passage discusses the effect of committing a major sin on the 'adalah of a witness. The ruling is that committing a major sin removes 'adalah, rendering the witness disqualified.
2
Recognize the definition dispute
Scholars differed on the definition of kabira. One view defines it as any sin incurring a hadd punishment. The Hanafis (ashabuna) rejected this and built their definition on three criteria.
The Hanafi definition is broader and more practical.
3
State the three Hanafi criteria for a major sin
First: an act that is abominable (shani') among Muslims and involves violation of sanctity (hatk hurmah). Second: opposition to manliness (muru'ah) and generosity (karam). Third: persistence (israr) on sins and immorality. Any act meeting one or more of these is a kabira.
Persistence turns minor sins into major ones.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi criticism and the relied-upon position
Fath al-Qadir criticized the three-criteria definition as unregulated. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position from al-Fatawa al-Sughra and Adab al-Qada' is that 'adalah is lost by committing a kabira, and for minor sins, persistence (ghalabah) elevates them to kabira. Additionally, the sin must be manifest (zuhur) for the ruling to apply.
Thus, a single minor sin without persistence does not disqualify.
5
Apply the definition to a concrete scenario
Example: A witness is known to occasionally backbite (ghibah). Backbiting is a minor sin in Hanafi fiqh. If it is only occasional and not persistent, his 'adalah remains intact. If he persists and it becomes habitual, he is disqualified.
This shows the practical importance of the persistence criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يرتكب ما يوجب الحد للفسق . ولو قال أو يرتكب كبيرة لكان أولى . واختلف العلماء في الكبيرة والصغيرة على أقوال بيناها في شرح المنار في قسم السنة. وفي نقل القول بأن الكبيرة ما فيه حد بنص الكتاب قال: وأصحابنا لم يأخذوا بذلك وإنما بنوا على ثلاثة معان: أحدها ما كان شنيعاً بين المسلمين وفيه هتك حرمة. والثاني أن يكون فيه منابذة المروءة والكرم فكل فعل يرفض المروءة والكرم فهو كبيرة. والثالث أن يكون مصراً على المعاصي والفجور ا هـ . وتعقبه في فتح القدير بأنه غير منضبط وغير صحيح. وما في الفتاوى الصغرى العدل من يجتنب الكبائر كلها حتى لو ارتكب كبيرة تسقط عدالته، وفي الصغائر العبرة للغلبة لتصير كبيرة حسن، ونقله عن أدب القضاء لعصام وعليه المعول غير أن الحكم بزوال العدالة بارتكاب الكبيرة يحتاج إلى الظهور فلذا شرط في شرب المحرم الإدمان ا هـ .
His statement: (or he commits what necessitates the hadd punishment due to sin. If he said 'or he commits a major sin' it would be better. The scholars differed on major and minor sin according to statements we have explained in Sharh al-Manar in the section on Sunnah. In the transmission of the statement that the major sin is that which entails a hadd punishment by the text of the Book, he said: Our companions did not take that; rather they based it on three meanings: one: what is abominable among the Muslims and involves violation of sanctity. Two: that it includes opposition to manliness (muru'ah) and generosity (karam), so every action that rejects muru'ah and karam is a major sin. Three: persistence on sins and immorality. In Fath al-Qadir he criticized this saying it is not regulated and not correct. In al-Fatawa al-Sughra: the just person is one who avoids all major sins, so if he commits a major sin his justice is removed; and in minor sins the consideration is for the predominant (ghalabah) to become a major sin, that is good. He transmitted it from Adab al-Qada' of 'Isam, and upon it is reliance. However, the judgment of removal of justice by committing a major sin requires manifestation, so he conditioned addiction in drinking the forbidden.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and discussion on the definition of a major sin (kabirah) and how it affects the 'adalah (justice) of a witness. The text notes that the author of the matn said 'commits what necessitates hadd punishment', but it would be better to say 'commits a major sin'. The scholars have differed on the definition of major and minor sins, as explained in Sharh al-Manar. One opinion defines major sin as that which incurs a hadd punishment based on the text of the Quran. However, the Hanafi scholars (ashabuna) did not adopt that definition. Instead, they built the definition on three criteria: first, what is considered abominable (shani') among Muslims and includes violation of sanctity (hatk hurmah). Second, what opposes manliness (muru'ah) and generosity (karam); any action that rejects these is a major sin. Third, persistence on sins and immorality (israr 'ala al-ma'asi wa al-fujur). The commentator in Fath al-Qadir criticized this three-part definition as unregulated and unsound. Then al-Fatawa al-Sughra is quoted: a just witness avoids all major sins; committing a major sin removes his justice. For minor sins, if the person becomes predominantly persistent, they become major sins. This view is transmitted from 'Isam's Adab al-Qada' and is the relied-upon view. However, the removal of justice by committing a major sin requires that the sin be manifest (zuhur), so for example, in drinking the forbidden, addiction is required. This shows that not every single sin automatically disqualifies a witness; there must be evidence of persistence or flagrancy.

Key Terms
الكبيرة (al-kabīrah): Major sin; defined by Hanafis using three criteria: abominable/htak hurmah, opposition to muru'ah/karam, or persistence.
العدالة (al-‘adālah): Justice of a witness; a prerequisite for testimony, lost by committing major sins or persistence on minor ones.
المروءة (al-murū'ah): Manliness, chivalry; actions that oppose it are considered major sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, which of the following does NOT constitute a major sin (kabirah) for the purpose of disqualifying a witness?
A) An action that is abominable among Muslims and violates sanctity
B) An action that contradicts manliness (muru'ah) and generosity
C) A single minor sin committed once without persistence
D) Persistence on sinful behavior (israr)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines a major sin by its social and moral gravity or persistence, and its commission (if manifest) removes a witness's 'adalah.

Witnesses Whose Testimony Is Rejected
Lists categories of rejected witnesses with exceptions and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify specific categories of witnesses whose testimony is rejected in Hanafi law and explain the conditions under which their testimony may be accepted.

Core Idea
A witness's testimony is rejected for manifest sin or disreputable profession; frequency and intent matter.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists numerous categories of people whose testimony is rejected due to their occupation or behavior. It covers professional roles like dancers and document forgers, as well as personal habits like frequent abuse or miserliness. The rulings are nuanced: some professions lead to blanket rejection, while others are accepted if the person is generally righteous. The passage also addresses how delaying obligatory acts affects a witness's integrity.

Guided Path
1
List the categories
Read through the passage and note all the specific types of people mentioned whose testimony is rejected. Group them by profession (e.g., dancer, gatecrasher) and behavior (e.g., frequent abuser, miser).
Memorize this list as a reference.
2
Identify exceptions
For each category, check if the passage mentions an exception. For example, the seller of shrouds is rejected only if he watches for death; otherwise accepted. Document writers are rejected if they write falsehoods, but accepted if righteousness prevails.
Exceptions often hinge on intent or frequency.
3
Understand the principle
The underlying principle is that a witness must have 'adalah (moral integrity). Any manifest major sin or disreputable profession that indicates moral laxity can disqualify. Frequency (e.g., frequent abuse) matters more than occasional slips.
The principle of 'adalah is central to Hanafi witness law.
4
Analyze the delayed obligations
The passage discusses how delaying obligatory acts affects 'adalah. Note the distinction between fixed-time obligations (prayer, fasting) and non-fixed (zakat, hajj). There is a difference of opinion on hajj: the fatwa is that delaying zakat voids 'adalah, but delaying hajj does not, though some say both do.
This shows the Hanafi care for practical distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس بذكر ما اطلعنا عليه من كلامهم فيما يسقطها مما لم يكن في الكتاب في الذخيرة والمحيط الإعانة على المعاصي والحث عليها كبيرة قالوا : ولا تقبل شهادة بائع الأكفان. وقيده شمس الأئمة السرخسي بما إذا ترصد لذلك العمل وإلا فتقبل لعدم تمنية الموت والطاعون. ولا تقبل شهادة الصكاكين لأنهم يكتبون بخلاف الواقع والصحيح قبولها إذا غلب عليهم الصلاح ولا تقبل شهادة الطفيلي والرقاص والمجازف في كلامه والمسخرة بلا خلاف، ولا تقبل شهادة من يشتم أهله ومماليكه كثيراً لا أحياناً، وكذا الشتام للحيوان كدابته، وأما في ديارنا فكثيراً يشتمون بائع الدابة فيقولون قطع الله يد من باعك، ولا من يحلف في كلامه كثيراً، ولا تقبل شهادة البخيل ؛ الكل من فتح القدير. والذي أخر الفرض بعد وجوبه إن كان له وقت معين كالصوم والصلاة بطلت عدالته إلا أن يكون لعذر، وإن لم يكن له وقت معين كالزكاة والحج اختلف الرواية فيه والمشايخ وذكر الخاصي عن فتاوى قاضيخان الفتوى على سقوطها في تأخير الزكاة من غير عذر بخلاف تأخير الحج ا هـ . وفي خزانة الأكمل : إذا أخر الزكاة والحج من غير عذر بطلت وبه وتمامه في شرح
There is no harm in mentioning what we have come across from their statements regarding what invalidates testimony, which was not in the book. In al-Dhakhirah and al-Muhit: assistance to sins and urging towards it is a major sin. They said: The testimony of a seller of shrouds is not accepted. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi restricted it to when he watches for that work, otherwise it is accepted because he does not hope for death and plague. The testimony of document writers (al-sakkakin) is not accepted because they write contrary to reality; the correct view is that it is accepted if righteousness prevails upon them. The testimony of a gatecrasher (al-tufayli), a dancer (al-raqqas), one who makes exaggerated statements (al-mujazif fi kalamihi), and a mimic (al-maskharah) is not accepted without dispute. The testimony of one who abuses his family and slaves frequently, not occasionally, is not accepted. Similarly, one who abuses animals like his mount. In our regions, they frequently abuse the seller of an animal, saying 'May Allah cut off the hand of the one who sold you.' And one who swears excessively in his speech is not accepted. The testimony of a miser is not accepted. All this is from Fath al-Qadir. As for one who delays an obligatory act after it becomes due: if it has a specified time like fasting and prayer, his justice is voided unless there is an excuse. If it does not have a specified time like zakat and hajj, the narration and the mashayikh differed. Al-Khasi mentioned from Fatawa Qadikhan that the fatwa is on its invalidation in delaying zakat without excuse, unlike delaying hajj. In Khizanat al-Akmal: if he delays zakat and hajj without excuse, it is voided. Its completion is in the commentary.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing specific individuals whose testimony is rejected in Hanafi law. It begins by noting that assisting and urging towards sin is a major sin. Then it enumerates examples: a seller of shrouds (who hopes for death) is rejected, but if he does not specifically watch for that work, his testimony is accepted (as per Sarakhsi). Document writers (al-sakkakin) who write false contracts are rejected, but if they are generally righteous, they can be accepted. The testimony of a gatecrasher (tufayli), a dancer (raqqas), one who exaggerates (mujazif), and a mimic (maskharah) are rejected without dispute. Frequent abusers of family, slaves, or animals are rejected, as well as those who swear excessively. A miser is also rejected. These are all from Fath al-Qadir. Then it discusses delaying obligatory acts: if the act has a specific time (like prayer and fasting), delaying without excuse voids justice. If it has no specific time (like zakat and hajj), there is a difference of narration: the fatwa from Fatawa Qadikhan is that delaying zakat without excuse voids justice, but not hajj. However, Khizanat al-Akmal says both void justice without excuse. The lesson shows that many social and professional behaviors can disqualify a witness, and the ruling depends on frequency, intent, and the nature of the act.

Key Terms
الطفيلي (al-ṭufaylī): Gatecrasher; one who attends feasts without invitation.
الرقاص (al-raqqāṣ): Dancer; professional dancer considered disreputable.
الصكاكين (al-ṣakkākīn): Document writers or scribes who write false deeds.
البخيل (al-bakhīl): Miser; one who withholds obligatory duties like zakat.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is rejected for manifest major sins or disreputable professions, but exceptions apply based on intent, frequency, and excuse; delaying certain obligations can also void 'adalah.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate

Major and Minor Sins: Their Criteria and Effect on Testimonial Probity
Ruling statement on conditions of adala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi criteria for a major sin and apply them to determine whether a witness's adala is removed.

Core Idea
Adala is lost by committing a major sin (by three criteria) or by persisting in minor sins until they become major.

A witness must be 'adl – a person of moral probity. But what exactly destroys that probity? The Hanafi school does not rely solely on prescribed punishments (hadd); rather, it defines major sin through three substantive criteria. This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq unpacks that definition and applies it to a series of concrete behaviors, from drinking wine to delaying obligatory acts. Understanding these criteria is essential for determining who may testify in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the matn's phrasing
The commentator notes that the original text says 'commits what necessitates hadd' but suggests 'commits a major sin' is better. Why? Because the Hanafi definition of major sin is broader than acts with a prescribed punishment.
The commentary is refining the matn.
2
Memorize the three Hanafi criteria for major sin
Our school defines kabira by: (1) something repugnant and violating sanctity among Muslims, (2) contrary to muru'a and generosity, or (3) persistence on sins and immorality.
This is from the text, quoting the three meanings.
3
Understand the effect of persistence on minor sins
A minor sin becomes major if one persists in it (ghalaba). A person who commits minor sins occasionally may retain adala, but habitual commission elevates the sin to major.
The text says 'al-ibra li al-ghalaba'.
4
Apply the requirement of manifestness (zuhur)
For a major sin to remove adala, it must be manifest. Thus, drinking wine only removes adala if the person is addicted (idman), because addiction makes it known.
The passage links zuhur to the condition of idman for wine.
5
Analyze the list of actions that remove adala
The text gives many examples: assisting in sins, selling shrouds (if done with intent), writing false documents, being a gatecrasher, dancer, etc., insulting family, cursing animals, excessive swearing, miserliness, delaying obligatory acts without excuse.
Each example can be tested against the three criteria or the persistence rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يرتكب ما يوجب الحد للفسق . ولو قال أو يرتكب كبيرة لكان أولى . واختلف العلماء في الكبيرة والصغيرة على أقوال بيناها في شرح المنار في قسم السنة. وفي نقل القول بأن الكبيرة ما فيه حد بنص الكتاب قال: وأصحابنا لم يأخذوا بذلك وإنما بنوا على ثلاثة معان: أحدها ما كان شنيعاً بين المسلمين وفيه هتك حرمة. والثاني أن يكون فيه منابذة المروءة والكرم فكل فعل يرفض المروءة والكرم فهو كبيرة. والثالث أن يكون مصراً على المعاصي والفجور ا هـ . وتعقبه في فتح القدير بأنه غير منضبط وغير صحيح. وما في الفتاوى الصغرى العدل من يجتنب الكبائر كلها حتى لو ارتكب كبيرة تسقط عدالته، وفي الصغائر العبرة للغلبة لتصير كبيرة حسن، ونقله عن أدب القضاء لعصام وعليه المعول غير أن الحكم بزوال العدالة بارتكاب الكبيرة يحتاج إلى الظهور فلذا شرط في شرب المحرم الإدمان ا هـ . ولا بأس بذكر ما اطلعنا عليه من كلامهم فيما يسقطها مما لم يكن في الكتاب في الذخيرة والمحيط الإعانة على المعاصي والحث عليها كبيرة قالوا : ولا تقبل شهادة بائع الأكفان. وقيده شمس الأئمة السرخسي بما إذا ترصد لذلك العمل وإلا فتقبل لعدم تمنية الموت والطاعون. ولا تقبل شهادة الصكاكين لأنهم يكتبون بخلاف الواقع والصحيح قبولها إذا غلب عليهم الصلاح ولا تقبل شهادة الطفيلي والرقاص والمجازف في كلامه والمسخرة بلا خلاف، ولا تقبل شهادة من يشتم أهله ومماليكه كثيراً لا أحياناً، وكذا الشتام للحيوان كدابته، وأما في ديارنا فكثيراً يشتمون بائع الدابة فيقولون قطع الله يد من باعك، ولا من يحلف في كلامه كثيراً، ولا تقبل شهادة البخيل ؛ الكل من فتح القدير. والذي أخر الفرض بعد وجوبه إن كان له وقت معين كالصوم والصلاة بطلت عدالته إلا أن يكون لعذر، وإن لم يكن له وقت معين كالزكاة والحج اختلف الرواية فيه والمشايخ وذكر الخاصي عن فتاوى قاضيخان الفتوى على سقوطها في تأخير الزكاة من غير عذر بخلاف تأخير الحج ا هـ . وفي خزانة الأكمل : إذا أخر الزكاة والحج من غير عذر بطلت وبه وتمامه في شرح نأخذ ا هـ
His statement: (Or he commits what necessitates the hadd for fisq). And if he said 'or he commits a major sin [kabira]' it would be better. And the scholars differed about major and minor sin according to statements we explained in Sharh al-Manar in the section of Sunnah. And it was criticized in Fath al-Qadir as being unregulated and unsound. And what is in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: the just person is one who avoids all major sins, so if he commits a major sin his adala falls; and for minor sins the consideration is the predominance [of committing them] so that it becomes a major sin, good. And he transmitted it from Adab al-Qada of 'Isam, and reliance is upon it, except that the judgment of removal of adala by committing a major sin requires manifestness [of the sin]; hence the condition of addiction [idman] in drinking what is prohibited, end quote. And there is no harm in mentioning what we have encountered from their statements regarding what removes it [adala] that was not in the book: in al-Dhakhira and al-Muhit: assisting in sins and inciting to them is a major sin. They said: And the testimony of a seller of shrouds is not accepted. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi restricted it to when he watches out for that work; otherwise it is accepted because he does not wish for death and plague. And the testimony of document writers [sakkakin] is not accepted because they write contrary to reality, and the correct [view] is that it is accepted if righteousness prevails upon them. And the testimony of a gatecrasher [tufayli], a dancer [raqqas], one who exaggerates in his speech [mujazif], and a jester [maskhara] is not accepted without dispute. And the testimony of one who insults his family and slaves frequently, not occasionally, is not accepted, and likewise one who curses an animal like his riding beast. And in our lands they often curse the seller of an animal saying 'may Allah cut off the hand of the one who sold you'. And the testimony of one who swears frequently in his speech is not accepted, nor that of a miser; all from Fath al-Qadir. And he who delays an obligatory act after its obligation, if it has a specified time like fasting and prayer, his adala is invalidated unless he has an excuse. And if it has no specified time like zakat and Hajj, the narration and the mashayikh differ. And al-Khasi mentioned from Fatawa Qadikhan: the fatwa is on its fall [i.e., adala is removed] in delaying zakat without excuse, contrary to delaying Hajj, end quote. And in Khizanat al-Akmal: if he delays zakat and Hajj without excuse, it is invalidated, and with it is the completion in the explanation. We take? end quote.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the conditions of adala (moral probity) required for testimony. It begins by addressing the matn's phrase 'or he commits what necessitates the hadd for fisq' and notes that specifying 'major sin' would be better. The Hanafi school defines major sin (kabira) not by the presence of a fixed punishment (hadd) but by three criteria: (1) repugnance and violation of sanctity among Muslims, (2) violation of manliness (muru'a) and generosity, and (3) persistence on sins. Fath al-Qadir criticizes this as unregulated. The text then cites al-Fatawa al-Sughra that a just person avoids all major sins; committing a major sin removes adala. For minor sins, persistence (ghalaba) makes them major. The removal of adala for major sins requires the sin to be manifest (zuhur), hence the condition of addiction for drinking wine. The text then lists specific actions that remove adala: assisting in sins, selling shrouds (with qualification), document writing (sakkakin) if they write what is not true, gatecrashing, dancing, exaggeration, jesting, insulting family/slaves/frequently, cursing animals, excessive swearing, and miserliness. It also discusses delaying obligatory acts: prayers and fasting with a fixed time remove adala unless excused; delaying zakat without excuse removes adala according to fatwa, but delaying Hajj does not according to Qadikhan, though Khizanat al-Akmal says both remove it. The passage thus provides a detailed list of behaviors that disqualify a witness.

Key Terms
عدالة (adala): Moral probity required for testimony; a witness must be 'adl, avoiding major sins and not persisting in minor sins.
كبيرة (kabira): Major sin; Hanafis define it as any sin that is repugnant, violates muru'a, or is persisted in, not merely one with a hadd penalty.
صغيرة (saghira): Minor sin; persistence on a minor sin makes it a major sin by the criterion of 'ghalaba'.
إدمان (idman): Addiction or habitual committing; for drinking alcohol, idman is required to remove adala because only habitual drinking is manifest.
ظهور (zuhur): Manifestness; a sin must be apparent or known for it to remove adala.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a basis for declaring an action a major sin (kabira) in the Hanafi school?
A) The action is repugnant among Muslims and violates sanctity
B) The action has a prescribed hadd punishment in the Quran
C) The action is contrary to muru'a and generosity
D) The action is persistently committed

Key Takeaway
Hanafi adala is lost by committing a major sin (defined by repugnance, violation of muru'a, or persistence) or by persisting in minor sins until they become major, with the condition that the sin be manifest.

When Does Singing Disqualify a Witness?
Determining when singing disqualifies a witness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for when singing removes a witness's adala and distinguish between professional and occasional singing.

Core Idea
Only a professional singer loses adala; private or wise singing does not disqualify a witness.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a common misunderstanding in Hanafi fiqh: not all singing or listening to singing makes a witness unreliable. The text explains that the term 'singer' in legal discourse refers specifically to one who makes singing a profession (hirfa). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating witness testimony in Islamic courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the effect of singing on the adala (moral probity) of a witness? The passage addresses when a witness who sings or listens to singing is rejected.
The ruling is about shahadat (testimony).
2
Understand the 'urf (customary usage)
The term 'singer' (mughenn) is understood in common usage as one who takes singing as a trade (hirfa) to earn wealth, just as 'tailor' or 'blacksmith' indicates a profession.
This is based on 'urf, not just literal meaning.
3
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between professional singing (hirfa) and occasional or private singing. Only the former removes adala. Singing for oneself to relieve loneliness does not affect adala.
Private singing: no harm, adala retained.
4
Note exceptions
Reciting poetry containing exhortation and wisdom is permissible by consensus and does not remove adala. Similarly, listening to such recitation is fine.
Content matters: wise poetry is not like frivolous singing.
5
Practical application
When evaluating a witness known to sing, first determine if singing is his profession (hirfa). If yes, his testimony is rejected. If he sings occasionally for himself or recites wise poetry, his testimony is accepted.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text

موافق لما قاله الإمام السرخسي فكان محتملاً لكل من القولين نعم ظاهره الإطلاق وقد يقال لفظة المغنين ظاهرة في أن المراد من اتخذه حرفة وعادة ثم رأيت في الفتح قال : إن اسم مغنية ومغن إنما هو في العرف لمن كان الغناء حرفته التي يكتسب بها المال ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل ما حرفة فلان أو ما صناعته يقال مغن كما يقال خياط وحداد إلى آخر كلامه وفي إيضاح الإصلاح : إنما قال يغني للناس أي. يسمعهم لأنه لو كان لإسماع نفسه حتى يزيل الوحشة عن نفسه من غير ظن أن غيره لا بأس به ولا يسقط عدالته في الصحيح .اهـ. وهكذا قال في شرح العيني ثم قال : وإن أنشد شعراً فيه وعظ وحكمة فهو جائز بالاتفاق الخ. ونحوه ما مر عن الفتح من قوله المحرم هو ما كان الخ» فتدبر قوله : (لأنهم يكتبون بخلاف الواقع قال في الخلاصة : لأنهم يكتبون هذا ما اشترى يسمع
It agrees with what Imam al-Sarakhsi said, so it is possible for each of the two statements. Yes, its apparent meaning is absolute, and it may be said that the term 'singers' [al-mughannin] apparently means the one who takes it as a trade and habit. Then I saw in al-Fath that he said: The name 'female singer' and 'singer' is only used customarily for one for whom singing is his trade by which he earns wealth. Do you not see that when it is said 'what is so-and-so's trade or what is his profession?', it is said 'a singer' just as it is said 'a tailor' and 'a blacksmith' to the end of his words. And in Idah al-Islah: He only said 'he sings for people' meaning he makes them hear, because if it is for hearing himself so that he removes loneliness from himself without suspicion that others [hear], then there is no harm in it and his adala is not removed in the correct view. End quote. And likewise it is said in Sharh al-Ayni, then he said: And if he recites poetry containing exhortation and wisdom, it is permissible by consensus. And similar to it is what passed from al-Fath from his statement: 'The prohibited is what...' etc. So reflect on his statement: (Because they write contrary to reality, he said in al-Khulasa: Because they write 'this is what he bought' so that it is heard).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq addressing the testimony of singers (mughennun) and those who listen to singing. The text begins by confirming agreement with Imam al-Sarakhsi that the term 'singers' is ambiguous but then explains that in common usage ('urf), a singer is one who has taken singing as a profession (hirfa) and habit, using it to earn money. This is supported by a quote from al-Fath: just as we say 'tailor' or 'blacksmith' as trades, similarly 'singer' implies a professional. The text then cites Idah al-Islah: singing for oneself to relieve loneliness without intention of others hearing does not remove adala. Similarly, reciting poetry with exhortation and wisdom is permissible by consensus. The passage ends with a note about document writers (sakkakin) who write 'this is what he bought' etc., though this seems like a separate point attached to the discussion. The key ruling is that only professional singers lose adala; occasional or private singing does not disqualify a witness.

Key Terms
مغن (mughenn): A singer; in legal context, one who takes singing as a profession (hirfa) to earn wealth.
حرفة (hirfa): Trade or profession; used to distinguish professional singers from occasional singers.
الغناء (al-ghina'): Singing; its prohibition and effect on adala depend on context: if for trade or causing fitna, it is prohibited; if for oneself or with wisdom, it may be permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following does NOT remove a witness's adala?
A) Singing as a profession to earn money
B) Singing for oneself to relieve loneliness
C) Reciting poetry with wisdom and exhortation
D) Listening to professional singers in markets

Key Takeaway
Only a professional singer loses adala; private singing or reciting wise poetry preserves the witness's moral probity.
